<div class="speech"><span class="ref">120</span> <div class="sentence">  Neither must we forget that our idea of space, like our other ideas, has a history.</div><div class="sentence"> The Homeric poems contain no word for it; even the later Greek philosophy has not the Kantian notion of space, but only the definite 'place' or 'the infinite.'</div><div class="sentence"> To Plato, in the Timaeus, it is known only as the 'nurse of generation.'</div><div class="sentence"> When therefore we speak of the necessity of our ideas of space we must remember that this is a necessity which has grown up with the growth of the human mind, and has been made by ourselves.</div><div class="sentence"> We can free ourselves from the perplexities which are involved in it by ascending to a time in which they did not as yet exist.</div><div class="sentence"> And when space or time are described as 'a priori forms or intuitions added to the matter given in sensation,' we should consider that such expressions belong really to the 'pre-historic study' of philosophy, i.e.</div><div class="sentence"> to the eighteenth century, when men sought to explain the human mind without regard to history or language or the social nature of man.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">121</span> <div class="sentence">  In every act of sense there is a latent perception of space, of which we only become conscious when objects are withdrawn from it.</div><div class="sentence"> There are various ways in which we may trace the connexion between them.</div><div class="sentence"> We may think of space as unresisting matter, and of matter as divided into objects; or of objects again as formed by abstraction into a collective notion of matter, and of matter as rarefied into space.</div><div class="sentence"> And motion may be conceived as the union of there and not there in space, and force as the materializing or solidification of motion.</div><div class="sentence"> Space again is the individual and universal in one; or, in other words, a perception and also a conception.</div><div class="sentence"> So easily do what are sometimes called our simple ideas pass into one another, and differences of kind resolve themselves into differences of degree.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">122</span> <div class="sentence">  Within or behind space there is another abstraction in many respects similar to it—time, the form of the inward, as space is the form of the outward.</div><div class="sentence"> As we cannot think of outward objects of sense or of outward sensations without space, so neither can we think of a succession of sensations without time.</div><div class="sentence"> It is the vacancy of thoughts or sensations, as space is the void of outward objects, and we can no more imagine the mind without the one than the world without the other.</div><div class="sentence"> It is to arithmetic what space is to geometry; or, more strictly, arithmetic may be said to be equally applicable to both.</div><div class="sentence"> It is defined in our minds, partly by the analogy of space and partly by the recollection of events which have happened to us, or the consciousness of feelings which we are experiencing.</div><div class="sentence"> Like space, it is without limit, for whatever beginning or end of time we fix, there is a beginning and end before them, and so on without end.</div><div class="sentence"> We speak of a past, present, and future, and again the analogy of space assists us in conceiving of them as coexistent.</div><div class="sentence"> When the limit of time is removed there arises in our minds the idea of eternity, which at first, like time itself, is only negative, but gradually, when connected with the world and the divine nature, like the other negative infinity of space, becomes positive.</div><div class="sentence"> Whether time is prior to the mind and to experience, or coeval with them, is (like the parallel question about space) unmeaning.</div><div class="sentence"> Like space it has been realized gradually: in the Homeric poems, or even in the Hesiodic cosmogony, there is no more notion of time than of space.</div><div class="sentence"> The conception of being is more general than either, and might therefore with greater plausibility be affirmed to be a condition or quality of the mind.</div><div class="sentence"> The a priori intuitions of Kant would have been as unintelligible to Plato as his a priori synthetical propositions to Aristotle.</div><div class="sentence"> The philosopher of Konigsberg supposed himself to be analyzing a necessary mode of thought: he was not aware that he was dealing with a mere abstraction.</div><div class="sentence"> But now that we are able to trace the gradual developement of ideas through religion, through language, through abstractions, why should we interpose the fiction of time between ourselves and realities?</div><div class="sentence"> Why should we single out one of these abstractions to be the a priori condition of all the others?</div><div class="sentence"> It comes last and not first in the order of our thoughts, and is not the condition precedent of them, but the last generalization of them.</div><div class="sentence"> Nor can any principle be imagined more suicidal to philosophy than to assume that all the truth which we are capable of attaining is seen only through an unreal medium.</div><div class="sentence"> If all that exists in time is illusion, we may well ask with Plato, 'What becomes of the mind?'</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">123</span> <div class="sentence">  Leaving the a priori conditions of sensation we may proceed to consider acts of sense.</div><div class="sentence"> These admit of various degrees of duration or intensity; they admit also of a greater or less extension from one object, which is perceived directly, to many which are perceived indirectly or in a less degree, and to the various associations of the object which are latent in the mind.</div><div class="sentence"> In general the greater the intension the less the extension of them.</div><div class="sentence"> The simplest sensation implies some relation of objects to one another, some position in space, some relation to a previous or subsequent sensation.</div><div class="sentence"> The acts of seeing and hearing may be almost unconscious and may pass away unnoted; they may also leave an impression behind them or power of recalling them.</div><div class="sentence"> If, after seeing an object we shut our eyes, the object remains dimly seen in the same or about the same place, but with form and lineaments half filled up.</div><div class="sentence"> This is the simplest act of memory.</div><div class="sentence"> And as we cannot see one thing without at the same time seeing another, different objects hang together in recollection, and when we call for one the other quickly follows.</div><div class="sentence"> To think of the place in which we have last seen a thing is often the best way of recalling it to the mind.</div><div class="sentence"> Hence memory is dependent on association.</div><div class="sentence"> The act of recollection may be compared to the sight of an object at a great distance which we have previously seen near and seek to bring near to us in thought.</div><div class="sentence"> Memory is to sense as dreaming is to waking; and like dreaming has a wayward and uncertain power of recalling impressions from the past.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">124</span> <div class="sentence">  Thus begins the passage from the outward to the inward sense.</div><div class="sentence"> But as yet there is no conception of a universal—the mind only remembers the individual object or objects, and is always attaching to them some colour or association of sense.</div><div class="sentence"> The power of recollection seems to depend on the intensity or largeness of the perception, or on the strength of some emotion with which it is inseparably connected.</div><div class="sentence"> This is the natural memory which is allied to sense, such as children appear to have and barbarians and animals.</div><div class="sentence"> It is necessarily limited in range, and its limitation is its strength.</div><div class="sentence"> In later life, when the mind has become crowded with names, acts, feelings, images innumerable, we acquire by education another memory of system and arrangement which is both stronger and weaker than the first—weaker in the recollection of sensible impressions as they are represented to us by eye or ear—stronger by the natural connexion of ideas with objects or with one another.</div><div class="sentence"> And many of the notions which form a part of the train of our thoughts are hardly realized by us at the time, but, like numbers or algebraical symbols, are used as signs only, thus lightening the labour of recollection.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">125</span> <div class="sentence">  And now we may suppose that numerous images present themselves to the mind, which begins to act upon them and to arrange them in various ways.</div><div class="sentence"> Besides the impression of external objects present with us or just absent from us, we have a dimmer conception of other objects which have disappeared from our immediate recollection and yet continue to exist in us.</div><div class="sentence"> The mind is full of fancies which are passing to and fro before it.</div><div class="sentence"> Some feeling or association calls them up, and they are uttered by the lips.</div><div class="sentence"> This is the first rudimentary imagination, which may be truly described in the language of Hobbes, as 'decaying sense,' an expression which may be applied with equal truth to memory as well.</div><div class="sentence"> For memory and imagination, though we sometimes oppose them, are nearly allied; the difference between them seems chiefly to lie in the activity of the one compared with the passivity of the other.</div><div class="sentence"> The sense decaying in memory receives a flash of light or life from imagination.</div><div class="sentence"> Dreaming is a link of connexion between them; for in dreaming we feebly recollect and also feebly imagine at one and the same time.</div><div class="sentence"> When reason is asleep the lower part of the mind wanders at will amid the images which have been received from without, the intelligent element retires, and the sensual or sensuous takes its place.</div><div class="sentence"> And so in the first efforts of imagination reason is latent or set aside; and images, in part disorderly, but also having a unity (however imperfect) of their own, pour like a flood over the mind.</div><div class="sentence"> And if we could penetrate into the heads of animals we should probably find that their intelligence, or the state of what in them is analogous to our intelligence, is of this nature.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">126</span> <div class="sentence">  Thus far we have been speaking of men, rather in the points in which they resemble animals than in the points in which they differ from them.</div><div class="sentence"> The animal too has memory in various degrees, and the elements of imagination, if, as appears to be the case, he dreams.</div><div class="sentence"> How far their powers or instincts are educated by the circumstances of their lives or by intercourse with one another or with mankind, we cannot precisely tell.</div><div class="sentence"> They, like ourselves, have the physical inheritance of form, scent, hearing, sight, and other qualities or instincts.</div><div class="sentence"> But they have not the mental inheritance of thoughts and ideas handed down by tradition, 'the slow additions that build up the mind' of the human race.</div><div class="sentence"> And language, which is the great educator of mankind, is wanting in them; whereas in us language is ever present—even in the infant the latent power of naming is almost immediately observable.</div><div class="sentence"> And therefore the description which has been already given of the nascent power of the faculties is in reality an anticipation.</div><div class="sentence"> For simultaneous with their growth in man a growth of language must be supposed.</div><div class="sentence"> The child of two years old sees the fire once and again, and the feeble observation of the same recurring object is associated with the feeble utterance of the name by which he is taught to call it.</div><div class="sentence"> Soon he learns to utter the name when the object is no longer there, but the desire or imagination of it is present to him.</div><div class="sentence"> At first in every use of the word there is a colour of sense, an indistinct picture of the object which accompanies it.</div><div class="sentence"> But in later years he sees in the name only the universal or class word, and the more abstract the notion becomes, the more vacant is the image which is presented to him.</div><div class="sentence"> Henceforward all the operations of his mind, including the perceptions of sense, are a synthesis of sensations, words, conceptions.</div><div class="sentence"> In seeing or hearing or looking or listening the sensible impression prevails over the conception and the word.</div><div class="sentence"> In reflection the process is reversed—the outward object fades away into nothingness, the name or the conception or both together are everything.</div><div class="sentence"> Language, like number, is intermediate between the two, partaking of the definiteness of the outer and of the universality of the inner world.</div><div class="sentence"> For logic teaches us that every word is really a universal, and only condescends by the help of position or circumlocution to become the expression of individuals or particulars.</div><div class="sentence"> And sometimes by using words as symbols we are able to give a 'local habitation and a name' to the infinite and inconceivable.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">127</span> <div class="sentence">  Thus we see that no line can be drawn between the powers of sense and of reflection—they pass imperceptibly into one another.</div><div class="sentence"> We may indeed distinguish between the seeing and the closed eye—between the sensation and the recollection of it.</div><div class="sentence"> But this distinction carries us a very little way, for recollection is present in sight as well as sight in recollection.</div><div class="sentence"> There is no impression of sense which does not simultaneously recall differences of form, number, colour, and the like.</div><div class="sentence"> Neither is such a distinction applicable at all to our internal bodily sensations, which give no sign of themselves when unaccompanied with pain, and even when we are most conscious of them, have often no assignable place in the human frame.</div><div class="sentence"> Who can divide the nerves or great nervous centres from the mind which uses them?</div><div class="sentence"> Who can separate the pains and pleasures of the mind from the pains and pleasures of the body?</div><div class="sentence"> The words 'inward and outward,' 'active and passive,' 'mind and body,' are best conceived by us as differences of degree passing into differences of kind, and at one time and under one aspect acting in harmony and then again opposed.</div><div class="sentence"> They introduce a system and order into the knowledge of our being; and yet, like many other general terms, are often in advance of our actual analysis or observation.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">128</span> <div class="sentence">  According to some writers the inward sense is only the fading away or imperfect realization of the outward.</div><div class="sentence"> But this leaves out of sight one half of the phenomenon.</div><div class="sentence"> For the mind is not only withdrawn from the world of sense but introduced to a higher world of thought and reflection, in which, like the outward sense, she is trained and educated.</div><div class="sentence"> By use the outward sense becomes keener and more intense, especially when confined within narrow limits.</div><div class="sentence"> The savage with little or no thought has a quicker discernment of the track than the civilised man; in like manner the dog, having the help of scent as well as of sight, is superior to the savage.</div><div class="sentence"> By use again the inward thought becomes more defined and distinct; what was at first an effort is made easy by the natural instrumentality of language, and the mind learns to grasp universals with no more exertion than is required for the sight of an outward object.</div><div class="sentence"> There is a natural connexion and arrangement of them, like the association of objects in a landscape.</div><div class="sentence"> Just as a note or two of music suffices to recall a whole piece to the musician's or composer's mind, so a great principle or leading thought suggests and arranges a world of particulars.</div><div class="sentence"> The power of reflection is not feebler than the faculty of sense, but of a higher and more comprehensive nature.</div><div class="sentence"> It not only receives the universals of sense, but gives them a new content by comparing and combining them with one another.</div><div class="sentence"> It withdraws from the seen that it may dwell in the unseen.</div><div class="sentence"> The sense only presents us with a flat and impenetrable surface: the mind takes the world to pieces and puts it together on a new pattern.</div><div class="sentence"> The universals which are detached from sense are reconstructed in science.</div><div class="sentence"> They and not the mere impressions of sense are the truth of the world in which we live; and (as an argument to those who will only believe 'what they can hold in their hands') we may further observe that they are the source of our power over it.</div><div class="sentence"> To say that the outward sense is stronger than the inward is like saying that the arm of the workman is stronger than the constructing or directing mind.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">129</span> <div class="sentence">  Returning to the senses we may briefly consider two questions—first their relation to the mind, secondly, their relation to outward objects:—</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">130</span> <div class="sentence">  1.</div><div class="sentence"> The senses are not merely 'holes set in a wooden horse' (Theaet.</div><div class="sentence"> ), but instruments of the mind with which they are organically connected.</div><div class="sentence"> There is no use of them without some use of words—some natural or latent logic—some previous experience or observation.</div><div class="sentence"> Sensation, like all other mental processes, is complex and relative, though apparently simple.</div><div class="sentence"> The senses mutually confirm and support one another; it is hard to say how much our impressions of hearing may be affected by those of sight, or how far our impressions of sight may be corrected by the touch, especially in infancy.</div><div class="sentence"> The confirmation of them by one another cannot of course be given by any one of them.</div><div class="sentence"> Many intuitions which are inseparable from the act of sense are really the result of complicated reasonings.</div><div class="sentence"> The most cursory glance at objects enables the experienced eye to judge approximately of their relations and distance, although nothing is impressed upon the retina except colour, including gradations of light and shade.</div><div class="sentence"> From these delicate and almost imperceptible differences we seem chiefly to derive our ideas of distance and position.</div><div class="sentence"> By comparison of what is near with what is distant we learn that the tree, house, river, etc.</div><div class="sentence"> which are a long way off are objects of a like nature with those which are seen by us in our immediate neighbourhood, although the actual impression made on the eye is very different in one case and in the other.</div><div class="sentence"> This is a language of 'large and small letters' (Republic), slightly differing in form and exquisitely graduated by distance, which we are learning all our life long, and which we attain in various degrees according to our powers of sight or observation.</div><div class="sentence"> There is nor the consideration.</div><div class="sentence"> The greater or less strain upon the nerves of the eye or ear is communicated to the mind and silently informs the judgment.</div><div class="sentence"> We have also the use not of one eye only, but of two, which give us a wider range, and help us to discern, by the greater or less acuteness of the angle which the rays of sight form, the distance of an object and its relation to other objects.</div><div class="sentence"> But we are already passing beyond the limits of our actual knowledge on a subject which has given rise to many conjectures.</div><div class="sentence"> More important than the addition of another conjecture is the observation, whether in the case of sight or of any other sense, of the great complexity of the causes and the great simplicity of the effect.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">131</span> <div class="sentence">  The sympathy of the mind and the ear is no less striking than the sympathy of the mind and the eye.</div><div class="sentence"> Do we not seem to perceive instinctively and as an act of sense the differences of articulate speech and of musical notes?</div><div class="sentence"> Yet how small a part of speech or of music is produced by the impression of the ear compared with that which is furnished by the mind!</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">132</span> <div class="sentence">  Again: the more refined faculty of sense, as in animals so also in man, seems often to be transmitted by inheritance.</div><div class="sentence"> Neither must we forget that in the use of the senses, as in his whole nature, man is a social being, who is always being educated by language, habit, and the teaching of other men as well as by his own observation.</div><div class="sentence"> He knows distance because he is taught it by a more experienced judgment than his own; he distinguishes sounds because he is told to remark them by a person of a more discerning ear.</div><div class="sentence"> And as we inherit from our parents or other ancestors peculiar powers of sense or feeling, so we improve and strengthen them, not only by regular teaching, but also by sympathy and communion with other persons.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">133</span> <div class="sentence">  2.</div><div class="sentence"> The second question, namely, that concerning the relation of the mind to external objects, is really a trifling one, though it has been made the subject of a famous philosophy.</div><div class="sentence"> We may if we like, with Berkeley, resolve objects of sense into sensations; but the change is one of name only, and nothing is gained and something is lost by such a resolution or confusion of them.</div><div class="sentence"> For we have not really made a single step towards idealism, and any arbitrary inversion of our ordinary modes of speech is disturbing to the mind.</div><div class="sentence"> The youthful metaphysician is delighted at his marvellous discovery that nothing is, and that what we see or feel is our sensation only: for a day or two the world has a new interest to him; he alone knows the secret which has been communicated to him by the philosopher, that mind is all—when in fact he is going out of his mind in the first intoxication of a great thought.</div><div class="sentence"> But he soon finds that all things remain as they were—the laws of motion, the properties of matter, the qualities of substances.</div><div class="sentence"> After having inflicted his theories on any one who is willing to receive them 'first on his father and mother, secondly on some other patient listener, thirdly on his dog,' he finds that he only differs from the rest of mankind in the use of a word.</div><div class="sentence"> He had once hoped that by getting rid of the solidity of matter he might open a passage to worlds beyond.</div><div class="sentence"> He liked to think of the world as the representation of the divine nature, and delighted to imagine angels and spirits wandering through space, present in the room in which he is sitting without coming through the door, nowhere and everywhere at the same instant.</div><div class="sentence"> At length he finds that he has been the victim of his own fancies; he has neither more nor less evidence of the supernatural than he had before.</div><div class="sentence"> He himself has become unsettled, but the laws of the world remain fixed as at the beginning.</div><div class="sentence"> He has discovered that his appeal to the fallibility of sense was really an illusion.</div><div class="sentence"> For whatever uncertainty there may be in the appearances of nature, arises only out of the imperfection or variation of the human senses, or possibly from the deficiency of certain branches of knowledge; when science is able to apply her tests, the uncertainty is at an end.</div><div class="sentence"> We are apt sometimes to think that moral and metaphysical philosophy are lowered by the influence which is exercised over them by physical science.</div><div class="sentence"> But any interpretation of nature by physical science is far in advance of such idealism.</div><div class="sentence"> The philosophy of Berkeley, while giving unbounded license to the imagination, is still grovelling on the level of sense.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">134</span> <div class="sentence">  We may, if we please, carry this scepticism a step further, and deny, not only objects of sense, but the continuity of our sensations themselves.</div><div class="sentence"> We may say with Protagoras and Hume that what is appears, and that what appears appears only to individuals, and to the same individual only at one instant.</div><div class="sentence"> But then, as Plato asks,—and we must repeat the question,—What becomes of the mind?</div><div class="sentence"> Experience tells us by a thousand proofs that our sensations of colour, taste, and the like, are the same as they were an instant ago—that the act which we are performing one minute is continued by us in the next—and also supplies abundant proof that the perceptions of other men are, speaking generally, the same or nearly the same with our own.</div><div class="sentence"> After having slowly and laboriously in the course of ages gained a conception of a whole and parts, of the constitution of the mind, of the relation of man to God and nature, imperfect indeed, but the best we can, we are asked to return again to the 'beggarly elements' of ancient scepticism, and acknowledge only atoms and sensations devoid of life or unity.</div><div class="sentence"> Why should we not go a step further still and doubt the existence of the senses of all things?</div><div class="sentence"> We are but 'such stuff as dreams are made of;' for we have left ourselves no instruments of thought by which we can distinguish man from the animals, or conceive of the existence even of a mollusc.</div><div class="sentence"> And observe, this extreme scepticism has been allowed to spring up among us, not, like the ancient scepticism, in an age when nature and language really seemed to be full of illusions, but in the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries, when men walk in the daylight of inductive science.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">135</span> <div class="sentence">  The attractiveness of such speculations arises out of their true nature not being perceived.</div><div class="sentence"> They are veiled in graceful language; they are not pushed to extremes; they stop where the human mind is disposed also to stop—short of a manifest absurdity.</div><div class="sentence"> Their inconsistency is not observed by their authors or by mankind in general, who are equally inconsistent themselves.</div><div class="sentence"> They leave on the mind a pleasing sense of wonder and novelty: in youth they seem to have a natural affinity to one class of persons as poetry has to another; but in later life either we drift back into common sense, or we make them the starting-points of a higher philosophy.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">136</span> <div class="sentence">  We are often told that we should enquire into all things before we accept them;—with what limitations is this true?</div><div class="sentence"> For we cannot use our senses without admitting that we have them, or think without presupposing that there is in us a power of thought, or affirm that all knowledge is derived from experience without implying that this first principle of knowledge is prior to experience.</div><div class="sentence"> The truth seems to be that we begin with the natural use of the mind as of the body, and we seek to describe this as well as we can.</div><div class="sentence"> We eat before we know the nature of digestion; we think before we know the nature of reflection.</div><div class="sentence"> As our knowledge increases, our perception of the mind enlarges also.</div><div class="sentence"> We cannot indeed get beyond facts, but neither can we draw any line which separates facts from ideas.</div><div class="sentence"> And the mind is not something separate from them but included in them, and they in the mind, both having a distinctness and individuality of their own.</div><div class="sentence"> To reduce our conception of mind to a succession of feelings and sensations is like the attempt to view a wide prospect by inches through a microscope, or to calculate a period of chronology by minutes.</div><div class="sentence"> The mind ceases to exist when it loses its continuity, which though far from being its highest determination, is yet necessary to any conception of it.</div><div class="sentence"> Even an inanimate nature cannot be adequately represented as an endless succession of states or conditions.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">137</span> <div class="sentence">  Paragraph II.</div><div class="sentence"> Another division of the subject has yet to be considered: Why should the doctrine that knowledge is sensation, in ancient times, or of sensationalism or materialism in modern times, be allied to the lower rather than to the higher view of ethical philosophy?</div><div class="sentence"> At first sight the nature and origin of knowledge appear to be wholly disconnected from ethics and religion, nor can we deny that the ancient Stoics were materialists, or that the materialist doctrines prevalent in modern times have been associated with great virtues, or that both religious and philosophical idealism have not unfrequently parted company with practice.</div><div class="sentence"> Still upon the whole it must be admitted that the higher standard of duty has gone hand in hand with the higher conception of knowledge.</div><div class="sentence"> It is Protagoras who is seeking to adapt himself to the opinions of the world; it is Plato who rises above them: the one maintaining that all knowledge is sensation; the other basing the virtues on the idea of good.</div><div class="sentence"> The reason of this phenomenon has now to be examined.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">138</span> <div class="sentence">  By those who rest knowledge immediately upon sense, that explanation of human action is deemed to be the truest which is nearest to sense.</div><div class="sentence"> As knowledge is reduced to sensation, so virtue is reduced to feeling, happiness or good to pleasure.</div><div class="sentence"> The different virtues—the various characters which exist in the world—are the disguises of self-interest.</div><div class="sentence"> Human nature is dried up; there is no place left for imagination, or in any higher sense for religion.</div><div class="sentence"> Ideals of a whole, or of a state, or of a law of duty, or of a divine perfection, are out of place in an Epicurean philosophy.</div><div class="sentence"> The very terms in which they are expressed are suspected of having no meaning.</div><div class="sentence"> Man is to bring himself back as far as he is able to the condition of a rational beast.</div><div class="sentence"> He is to limit himself to the pursuit of pleasure, but of this he is to make a far-sighted calculation;—he is to be rationalized, secularized, animalized: or he is to be an amiable sceptic, better than his own philosophy, and not falling below the opinions of the world.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">139</span> <div class="sentence">  Imagination has been called that 'busy faculty' which is always intruding upon us in the search after truth.</div><div class="sentence"> But imagination is also that higher power by which we rise above ourselves and the commonplaces of thought and life.</div><div class="sentence"> The philosophical imagination is another name for reason finding an expression of herself in the outward world.</div><div class="sentence"> To deprive life of ideals is to deprive it of all higher and comprehensive aims and of the power of imparting and communicating them to others.</div><div class="sentence"> For men are taught, not by those who are on a level with them, but by those who rise above them, who see the distant hills, who soar into the empyrean.</div><div class="sentence"> Like a bird in a cage, the mind confined to sense is always being brought back from the higher to the lower, from the wider to the narrower view of human knowledge.</div><div class="sentence"> It seeks to fly but cannot: instead of aspiring towards perfection, 'it hovers about this lower world and the earthly nature.'</div><div class="sentence"> It loses the religious sense which more than any other seems to take a man out of himself.</div><div class="sentence"> Weary of asking 'What is truth?'</div><div class="sentence"> it accepts the 'blind witness of eyes and ears;' it draws around itself the curtain of the physical world and is satisfied.</div><div class="sentence"> The strength of a sensational philosophy lies in the ready accommodation of it to the minds of men; many who have been metaphysicians in their youth, as they advance in years are prone to acquiesce in things as they are, or rather appear to be.</div><div class="sentence"> They are spectators, not thinkers, and the best philosophy is that which requires of them the least amount of mental effort.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">140</span> <div class="sentence">  As a lower philosophy is easier to apprehend than a higher, so a lower way of life is easier to follow; and therefore such a philosophy seems to derive a support from the general practice of mankind.</div><div class="sentence"> It appeals to principles which they all know and recognize: it gives back to them in a generalized form the results of their own experience.</div><div class="sentence"> To the man of the world they are the quintessence of his own reflections upon life.</div><div class="sentence"> To follow custom, to have no new ideas or opinions, not to be straining after impossibilities, to enjoy to-day with just so much forethought as is necessary to provide for the morrow, this is regarded by the greater part of the world as the natural way of passing through existence.</div><div class="sentence"> And many who have lived thus have attained to a lower kind of happiness or equanimity.</div><div class="sentence"> They have possessed their souls in peace without ever allowing them to wander into the region of religious or political controversy, and without any care for the higher interests of man.</div><div class="sentence"> But nearly all the good (as well as some of the evil) which has ever been done in this world has been the work of another spirit, the work of enthusiasts and idealists, of apostles and martyrs.</div><div class="sentence"> The leaders of mankind have not been of the gentle Epicurean type; they have personified ideas; they have sometimes also been the victims of them.</div><div class="sentence"> But they have always been seeking after a truth or ideal of which they fell short; and have died in a manner disappointed of their hopes that they might lift the human race out of the slough in which they found them.</div><div class="sentence"> They have done little compared with their own visions and aspirations; but they have done that little, only because they sought to do, and once perhaps thought that they were doing, a great deal more.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">141</span> <div class="sentence">  The philosophies of Epicurus or Hume give no adequate or dignified conception of the mind.</div><div class="sentence"> There is no organic unity in a succession of feeling or sensations; no comprehensiveness in an infinity of separate actions.</div><div class="sentence"> The individual never reflects upon himself as a whole; he can hardly regard one act or part of his life as the cause or effect of any other act or part.</div><div class="sentence"> Whether in practice or speculation, he is to himself only in successive instants.</div><div class="sentence"> To such thinkers, whether in ancient or in modern times, the mind is only the poor recipient of impressions—not the heir of all the ages, or connected with all other minds.</div><div class="sentence"> It begins again with its own modicum of experience having only such vague conceptions of the wisdom of the past as are inseparable from language and popular opinion.</div><div class="sentence"> It seeks to explain from the experience of the individual what can only be learned from the history of the world.</div><div class="sentence"> It has no conception of obligation, duty, conscience—these are to the Epicurean or Utilitarian philosopher only names which interfere with our natural perceptions of pleasure and pain.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">142</span> <div class="sentence">  There seem then to be several answers to the question, Why the theory that all knowledge is sensation is allied to the lower rather than to the higher view of ethical philosophy:—1st, Because it is easier to understand and practise; 2ndly, Because it is fatal to the pursuit of ideals, moral, political, or religious; 3rdly, Because it deprives us of the means and instruments of higher thought, of any adequate conception of the mind, of knowledge, of conscience, of moral obligation.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">143</span> <div class="sentence">  ...</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">144</span> <div class="sentence">  ON THE NATURE AND LIMITS Of PSYCHOLOGY.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">145</span> <div class="sentence">  Since the above essay first appeared, many books on Psychology have been given to the world, partly based upon the views of Herbart and other German philosophers, partly independent of them.</div><div class="sentence"> The subject has gained in bulk and extent; whether it has had any true growth is more doubtful.</div><div class="sentence"> It begins to assume the language and claim the authority of a science; but it is only an hypothesis or outline, which may be filled up in many ways according to the fancy of individual thinkers.</div><div class="sentence"> The basis of it is a precarious one,—consciousness of ourselves and a somewhat uncertain observation of the rest of mankind.</div><div class="sentence"> Its relations to other sciences are not yet determined: they seem to be almost too complicated to be ascertained.</div><div class="sentence"> It may be compared to an irregular building, run up hastily and not likely to last, because its foundations are weak, and in many places rest only on the surface of the ground.</div><div class="sentence"> It has sought rather to put together scattered observations and to make them into a system than to describe or prove them.</div><div class="sentence"> It has never severely drawn the line between facts and opinions.</div><div class="sentence"> It has substituted a technical phraseology for the common use of language, being neither able to win acceptance for the one nor to get rid of the other.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">146</span> <div class="sentence">  The system which has thus arisen appears to be a kind of metaphysic narrowed to the point of view of the individual mind, through which, as through some new optical instrument limiting the sphere of vision, the interior of thought and sensation is examined.</div><div class="sentence"> But the individual mind in the abstract, as distinct from the mind of a particular individual and separated from the environment of circumstances, is a fiction only.</div><div class="sentence"> Yet facts which are partly true gather around this fiction and are naturally described by the help of it.</div><div class="sentence"> There is also a common type of the mind which is derived from the comparison of many minds with one another and with our own.</div><div class="sentence"> The phenomena of which Psychology treats are familiar to us, but they are for the most part indefinite; they relate to a something inside the body, which seems also to overleap the limits of space.</div><div class="sentence"> The operations of this something, when isolated, cannot be analyzed by us or subjected to observation and experiment.</div><div class="sentence"> And there is another point to be considered.</div><div class="sentence"> The mind, when thinking, cannot survey that part of itself which is used in thought.</div><div class="sentence"> It can only be contemplated in the past, that is to say, in the history of the individual or of the world.</div><div class="sentence"> This is the scientific method of studying the mind.</div><div class="sentence"> But Psychology has also some other supports, specious rather than real.</div><div class="sentence"> It is partly sustained by the false analogy of Physical Science and has great expectations from its near relationship to Physiology.</div><div class="sentence"> We truly remark that there is an infinite complexity of the body corresponding to the infinite subtlety of the mind; we are conscious that they are very nearly connected.</div><div class="sentence"> But in endeavouring to trace the nature of the connexion we are baffled and disappointed.</div><div class="sentence"> In our knowledge of them the gulf remains the same: no microscope has ever seen into thought; no reflection on ourselves has supplied the missing link between mind and matter...These are the conditions of this very inexact science, and we shall only know less of it by pretending to know more, or by assigning to it a form or style to which it has not yet attained and is not really entitled.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">147</span> <div class="sentence">  Experience shows that any system, however baseless and ineffectual, in our own or in any other age, may be accepted and continue to be studied, if it seeks to satisfy some unanswered question or is based upon some ancient tradition, especially if it takes the form and uses the language of inductive philosophy.</div><div class="sentence"> The fact therefore that such a science exists and is popular, affords no evidence of its truth or value.</div><div class="sentence"> Many who have pursued it far into detail have never examined the foundations on which it rests.</div><div class="sentence"> The have been many imaginary subjects of knowledge of which enthusiastic persons have made a lifelong study, without ever asking themselves what is the evidence for them, what is the use of them, how long they will last?</div><div class="sentence"> They may pass away, like the authors of them, and 'leave not a wrack behind;' or they may survive in fragments.</div><div class="sentence"> Nor is it only in the Middle Ages, or in the literary desert of China or of India, that such systems have arisen; in our own enlightened age, growing up by the side of Physics, Ethics, and other really progressive sciences, there is a weary waste of knowledge, falsely so-called.</div><div class="sentence"> There are sham sciences which no logic has ever put to the test, in which the desire for knowledge invents the materials of it.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">148</span> <div class="sentence">  And therefore it is expedient once more to review the bases of Psychology, lest we should be imposed upon by its pretensions.</div><div class="sentence"> The study of it may have done good service by awakening us to the sense of inveterate errors familiarized by language, yet it may have fallen into still greater ones; under the pretence of new investigations it may be wasting the lives of those who are engaged in it.</div><div class="sentence"> It may also be found that the discussion of it will throw light upon some points in the Theaetetus of Plato,—the oldest work on Psychology which has come down to us.</div><div class="sentence"> The imaginary science may be called, in the language of ancient philosophy, 'a shadow of a part of Dialectic or Metaphysic' (Gorg.</div><div class="sentence"> ).</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">149</span> <div class="sentence">  In this postscript or appendix we propose to treat, first, of the true bases of Psychology; secondly, of the errors into which the students of it are most likely to fall; thirdly, of the principal subjects which are usually comprehended under it; fourthly, of the form which facts relating to the mind most naturally assume.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">150</span> <div class="sentence">  We may preface the enquiry by two or three remarks:—</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">151</span> <div class="sentence">  (1) We do not claim for the popular Psychology the position of a science at all; it cannot, like the Physical Sciences, proceed by the Inductive Method: it has not the necessity of Mathematics: it does not, like Metaphysic, argue from abstract notions or from internal coherence.</div><div class="sentence"> It is made up of scattered observations.</div><div class="sentence"> A few of these, though they may sometimes appear to be truisms, are of the greatest value, and free from all doubt.</div><div class="sentence"> We are conscious of them in ourselves; we observe them working in others; we are assured of them at all times.</div><div class="sentence"> For example, we are absolutely certain, (a) of the influence exerted by the mind over the body or by the body over the mind: (b) of the power of association, by which the appearance of some person or the occurrence of some event recalls to mind, not always but often, other persons and events: (c) of the effect of habit, which is strongest when least disturbed by reflection, and is to the mind what the bones are to the body: (d) of the real, though not unlimited, freedom of the human will: (e) of the reference, more or less distinct, of our sensations, feelings, thoughts, actions, to ourselves, which is called consciousness, or, when in excess, self-consciousness: (f) of the distinction of the 'I' and 'Not I,' of ourselves and outward objects.</div><div class="sentence"> But when we attempt to gather up these elements in a single system, we discover that the links by which we combine them are apt to be mere words.</div><div class="sentence"> We are in a country which has never been cleared or surveyed; here and there only does a gleam of light come through the darkness of the forest.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">152</span> <div class="sentence">  (2) These fragments, although they can never become science in the ordinary sense of the word, are a real part of knowledge and may be of great value in education.</div><div class="sentence"> We may be able to add a good deal to them from our own experience, and we may verify them by it.</div><div class="sentence"> Self-examination is one of those studies which a man can pursue alone, by attention to himself and the processes of his individual mind.</div><div class="sentence"> He may learn much about his own character and about the character of others, if he will 'make his mind sit down' and look at itself in the glass.</div><div class="sentence"> The great, if not the only use of such a study is a practical one,—to know, first, human nature, and, secondly, our own nature, as it truly is.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">153</span> <div class="sentence">  (3) Hence it is important that we should conceive of the mind in the noblest and simplest manner.</div><div class="sentence"> While acknowledging that language has been the greatest factor in the formation of human thought, we must endeavour to get rid of the disguises, oppositions, contradictions, which arise out of it.</div><div class="sentence"> We must disengage ourselves from the ideas which the customary use of words has implanted in us.</div><div class="sentence"> To avoid error as much as possible when we are speaking of things unseen, the principal terms which we use should be few, and we should not allow ourselves to be enslaved by them.</div><div class="sentence"> Instead of seeking to frame a technical language, we should vary our forms of speech, lest they should degenerate into formulas.</div><div class="sentence"> A difficult philosophical problem is better understood when translated into the vernacular.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">154</span> <div class="sentence">  I.a.</div><div class="sentence"> Psychology is inseparable from language, and early language contains the first impressions or the oldest experience of man respecting himself.</div><div class="sentence"> These impressions are not accurate representations of the truth; they are the reflections of a rudimentary age of philosophy.</div><div class="sentence"> The first and simplest forms of thought are rooted so deep in human nature that they can never be got rid of; but they have been perpetually enlarged and elevated, and the use of many words has been transferred from the body to the mind.</div><div class="sentence"> The spiritual and intellectual have thus become separated from the material—there is a cleft between them; and the heart and the conscience of man rise above the dominion of the appetites and create a new language in which they too find expression.</div><div class="sentence"> As the differences of actions begin to be perceived, more and more names are needed.</div><div class="sentence"> This is the first analysis of the human mind; having a general foundation in popular experience, it is moulded to a certain extent by hierophants and philosophers.</div><div class="sentence"> (See Introd.</div><div class="sentence"> to Cratylus.)</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">155</span> <div class="sentence">  b.</div><div class="sentence"> This primitive psychology is continually receiving additions from the first thinkers, who in return take a colour from the popular language of the time.</div><div class="sentence"> The mind is regarded from new points of view, and becomes adapted to new conditions of knowledge.</div><div class="sentence"> It seeks to isolate itself from matter and sense, and to assert its independence in thought.</div><div class="sentence"> It recognizes that it is independent of the external world.</div><div class="sentence"> It has five or six natural states or stages:—(1) sensation, in which it is almost latent or quiescent: (2) feeling, or inner sense, when the mind is just awakening: (3) memory, which is decaying sense, and from time to time, as with a spark or flash, has the power of recollecting or reanimating the buried past: (4) thought, in which images pass into abstract notions or are intermingled with them: (5) action, in which the mind moves forward, of itself, or under the impulse of want or desire or pain, to attain or avoid some end or consequence: and (6) there is the composition of these or the admixture or assimilation of them in various degrees.</div><div class="sentence"> We never see these processes of the mind, nor can we tell the causes of them.</div><div class="sentence"> But we know them by their results, and learn from other men that so far as we can describe to them or they to us the workings of the mind, their experience is the same or nearly the same with our own.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">156</span> <div class="sentence">  c. But the knowledge of the mind is not to any great extent derived from the observation of the individual by himself.</div><div class="sentence"> It is the growing consciousness of the human race, embodied in language, acknowledged by experience, and corrected from time to time by the influence of literature and philosophy.</div><div class="sentence"> A great, perhaps the most important, part of it is to be found in early Greek thought.</div><div class="sentence"> In the Theaetetus of Plato it has not yet become fixed: we are still stumbling on the threshold.</div><div class="sentence"> In Aristotle the process is more nearly completed, and has gained innumerable abstractions, of which many have had to be thrown away because relative only to the controversies of the time.</div><div class="sentence"> In the interval between Thales and Aristotle were realized the distinctions of mind and body, of universal and particular, of infinite and infinitesimal, of idea and phenomenon; the class conceptions of faculties and virtues, the antagonism of the appetites and the reason; and connected with this, at a higher stage of development, the opposition of moral and intellectual virtue; also the primitive conceptions of unity, being, rest, motion, and the like.</div><div class="sentence"> These divisions were not really scientific, but rather based on popular experience.</div><div class="sentence"> They were not held with the precision of modern thinkers, but taken all together they gave a new existence to the mind in thought, and greatly enlarged and more accurately defined man's knowledge of himself and of the world.</div><div class="sentence"> The majority of them have been accepted by Christian and Western nations.</div><div class="sentence"> Yet in modern times we have also drifted so far away from Aristotle, that if we were to frame a system on his lines we should be at war with ordinary language and untrue to our own consciousness.</div><div class="sentence"> And there have been a few both in mediaeval times and since the Reformation who have rebelled against the Aristotelian point of view.</div><div class="sentence"> Of these eccentric thinkers there have been various types, but they have all a family likeness.</div><div class="sentence"> According to them, there has been too much analysis and too little synthesis, too much division of the mind into parts and too little conception of it as a whole or in its relation to God and the laws of the universe.</div><div class="sentence"> They have thought that the elements of plurality and unity have not been duly adjusted.</div><div class="sentence"> The tendency of such writers has been to allow the personality of man to be absorbed in the universal, or in the divine nature, and to deny the distinction between matter and mind, or to substitute one for the other.</div><div class="sentence"> They have broken some of the idols of Psychology: they have challenged the received meaning of words: they have regarded the mind under many points of view.</div><div class="sentence"> But though they may have shaken the old, they have not established the new; their views of philosophy, which seem like the echo of some voice from the East, have been alien to the mind of Europe.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">157</span> <div class="sentence">  d. The Psychology which is found in common language is in some degree verified by experience, but not in such a manner as to give it the character of an exact science.</div><div class="sentence"> We cannot say that words always correspond to facts.</div><div class="sentence"> Common language represents the mind from different and even opposite points of view, which cannot be all of them equally true (compare Cratylus).</div><div class="sentence"> Yet from diversity of statements and opinions may be obtained a nearer approach to the truth than is to be gained from any one of them.</div><div class="sentence"> It also tends to correct itself, because it is gradually brought nearer to the common sense of mankind.</div><div class="sentence"> There are some leading categories or classifications of thought, which, though unverified, must always remain the elements from which the science or study of the mind proceeds.</div><div class="sentence"> For example, we must assume ideas before we can analyze them, and also a continuing mind to which they belong; the resolution of it into successive moments, which would say, with Protagoras, that the man is not the same person which he was a minute ago, is, as Plato implies in the Theaetetus, an absurdity.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">158</span> <div class="sentence">  e. The growth of the mind, which may be traced in the histories of religions and philosophies and in the thoughts of nations, is one of the deepest and noblest modes of studying it.</div><div class="sentence"> Here we are dealing with the reality, with the greater and, as it may be termed, the most sacred part of history.</div><div class="sentence"> We study the mind of man as it begins to be inspired by a human or divine reason, as it is modified by circumstances, as it is distributed in nations, as it is renovated by great movements, which go beyond the limits of nations and affect human society on a scale still greater, as it is created or renewed by great minds, who, looking down from above, have a wider and more comprehensive vision.</div><div class="sentence"> This is an ambitious study, of which most of us rather 'entertain conjecture' than arrive at any detailed or accurate knowledge.</div><div class="sentence"> Later arises the reflection how these great ideas or movements of the world have been appropriated by the multitude and found a way to the minds of individuals.</div><div class="sentence"> The real Psychology is that which shows how the increasing knowledge of nature and the increasing experience of life have always been slowly transforming the mind, how religions too have been modified in the course of ages 'that God may be all and in all.'</div><div class="sentence"> E pollaplasion, eoe, to ergon e os nun zeteitai prostatteis.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">159</span> <div class="sentence">  f. Lastly, though we speak of the study of mind in a special sense, it may also be said that there is no science which does not contribute to our knowledge of it.</div><div class="sentence"> The methods of science and their analogies are new faculties, discovered by the few and imparted to the many.</div><div class="sentence"> They are to the mind, what the senses are to the body; or better, they may be compared to instruments such as the telescope or microscope by which the discriminating power of the senses, or to other mechanical inventions, by which the strength and skill of the human body is so immeasurably increased.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">160</span> <div class="sentence">  II.</div><div class="sentence"> The new Psychology, whatever may be its claim to the authority of a science, has called attention to many facts and corrected many errors, which without it would have been unexamined.</div><div class="sentence"> Yet it is also itself very liable to illusion.</div><div class="sentence"> The evidence on which it rests is vague and indefinite.</div><div class="sentence"> The field of consciousness is never seen by us as a whole, but only at particular points, which are always changing.</div><div class="sentence"> The veil of language intercepts facts.</div><div class="sentence"> Hence it is desirable that in making an approach to the study we should consider at the outset what are the kinds of error which most easily affect it, and note the differences which separate it from other branches of knowledge.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">161</span> <div class="sentence">  a.</div><div class="sentence"> First, we observe the mind by the mind.</div><div class="sentence"> It would seem therefore that we are always in danger of leaving out the half of that which is the subject of our enquiry.</div><div class="sentence"> We come at once upon the difficulty of what is the meaning of the word.</div><div class="sentence"> Does it differ as subject and object in the same manner?</div><div class="sentence"> Can we suppose one set of feelings or one part of the mind to interpret another?</div><div class="sentence"> Is the introspecting thought the same with the thought which is introspected?</div><div class="sentence"> Has the mind the power of surveying its whole domain at one and the same time?—No more than the eye can take in the whole human body at a glance.</div><div class="sentence"> Yet there may be a glimpse round the corner, or a thought transferred in a moment from one point of view to another, which enables us to see nearly the whole, if not at once, at any rate in succession.</div><div class="sentence"> Such glimpses will hardly enable us to contemplate from within the mind in its true proportions.</div><div class="sentence"> Hence the firmer ground of Psychology is not the consciousness of inward feelings but the observation of external actions, being the actions not only of ourselves, but of the innumerable persons whom we come across in life.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">162</span> <div class="sentence">  b.</div><div class="sentence"> The error of supposing partial or occasional explanation of mental phenomena to be the only or complete ones.</div><div class="sentence"> For example, we are disinclined to admit of the spontaneity or discontinuity of the mind—it seems to us like an effect without a cause, and therefore we suppose the train of our thoughts to be always called up by association.</div><div class="sentence"> Yet it is probable, or indeed certain, that of many mental phenomena there are no mental antecedents, but only bodily ones.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">163</span> <div class="sentence">  c. The false influence of language.</div><div class="sentence"> We are apt to suppose that when there are two or more words describing faculties or processes of the mind, there are real differences corresponding to them.</div><div class="sentence"> But this is not the case.</div><div class="sentence"> Nor can we determine how far they do or do not exist, or by what degree or kind of difference they are distinguished.</div><div class="sentence"> The same remark may be made about figures of speech.</div><div class="sentence"> They fill up the vacancy of knowledge; they are to the mind what too much colour is to the eye; but the truth is rather concealed than revealed by them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">164</span> <div class="sentence">  d. The uncertain meaning of terms, such as Consciousness, Conscience, Will, Law, Knowledge, Internal and External Sense; these, in the language of Plato, 'we shamelessly use, without ever having taken the pains to analyze them.'</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">165</span> <div class="sentence">  e. A science such as Psychology is not merely an hypothesis, but an hypothesis which, unlike the hypotheses of Physics, can never be verified.</div><div class="sentence"> It rests only on the general impressions of mankind, and there is little or no hope of adding in any considerable degree to our stock of mental facts.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">166</span> <div class="sentence">  f. The parallelism of the Physical Sciences, which leads us to analyze the mind on the analogy of the body, and so to reduce mental operations to the level of bodily ones, or to confound one with the other.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">167</span> <div class="sentence">  g. That the progress of Physiology may throw a new light on Psychology is a dream in which scientific men are always tempted to indulge.</div><div class="sentence"> But however certain we may be of the connexion between mind and body, the explanation of the one by the other is a hidden place of nature which has hitherto been investigated with little or no success.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">168</span> <div class="sentence">  h. The impossibility of distinguishing between mind and body.</div><div class="sentence"> Neither in thought nor in experience can we separate them.</div><div class="sentence"> They seem to act together; yet we feel that we are sometimes under the dominion of the one, sometimes of the other, and sometimes, both in the common use of language and in fact, they transform themselves, the one into the good principle, the other into the evil principle; and then again the 'I' comes in and mediates between them.</div><div class="sentence"> It is also difficult to distinguish outward facts from the ideas of them in the mind, or to separate the external stimulus to a sensation from the activity of the organ, or this from the invisible agencies by which it reaches the mind, or any process of sense from its mental antecedent, or any mental energy from its nervous expression.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">169</span> <div class="sentence">  i.</div><div class="sentence"> The fact that mental divisions tend to run into one another, and that in speaking of the mind we cannot always distinguish differences of kind from differences of degree; nor have we any measure of the strength and intensity of our ideas or feelings.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">170</span> <div class="sentence">  j.</div><div class="sentence"> Although heredity has been always known to the ancients as well as ourselves to exercise a considerable influence on human character, yet we are unable to calculate what proportion this birth-influence bears to nurture and education.</div><div class="sentence"> But this is the real question.</div><div class="sentence"> We cannot pursue the mind into embryology: we can only trace how, after birth, it begins to grow.</div><div class="sentence"> But how much is due to the soil, how much to the original latent seed, it is impossible to distinguish.</div><div class="sentence"> And because we are certain that heredity exercises a considerable, but undefined influence, we must not increase the wonder by exaggerating it.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">171</span> <div class="sentence">  k. The love of system is always tending to prevail over the historical investigation of the mind, which is our chief means of knowing it.</div><div class="sentence"> It equally tends to hinder the other great source of our knowledge of the mind, the observation of its workings and processes which we can make for ourselves.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">172</span> <div class="sentence">  l. The mind, when studied through the individual, is apt to be isolated—this is due to the very form of the enquiry; whereas, in truth, it is indistinguishable from circumstances, the very language which it uses being the result of the instincts of long-forgotten generations, and every word which a man utters being the answer to some other word spoken or suggested by somebody else.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">173</span> <div class="sentence">  III.</div><div class="sentence"> The tendency of the preceding remarks has been to show that Psychology is necessarily a fragment, and is not and cannot be a connected system.</div><div class="sentence"> We cannot define or limit the mind, but we can describe it.</div><div class="sentence"> We can collect information about it; we can enumerate the principal subjects which are included in the study of it.</div><div class="sentence"> Thus we are able to rehabilitate Psychology to some extent, not as a branch of science, but as a collection of facts bearing on human life, as a part of the history of philosophy, as an aspect of Metaphysic.</div><div class="sentence"> It is a fragment of a science only, which in all probability can never make any great progress or attain to much clearness or exactness.</div><div class="sentence"> It is however a kind of knowledge which has a great interest for us and is always present to us, and of which we carry about the materials in our own bosoms.</div><div class="sentence"> We can observe our minds and we can experiment upon them, and the knowledge thus acquired is not easily forgotten, and is a help to us in study as well as in conduct.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">174</span> <div class="sentence">  The principal subjects of Psychology may be summed up as follows:—</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">175</span> <div class="sentence">  a.</div><div class="sentence"> The relation of man to the world around him,—in what sense and within what limits can he withdraw from its laws or assert himself against them (Freedom and Necessity), and what is that which we suppose to be thus independent and which we call ourselves?</div><div class="sentence"> How does the inward differ from the outward and what is the relation between them, and where do we draw the line by which we separate mind from matter, the soul from the body?</div><div class="sentence"> Is the mind active or passive, or partly both?</div><div class="sentence"> Are its movements identical with those of the body, or only preconcerted and coincident with them, or is one simply an aspect of the other?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">176</span> <div class="sentence">  b.</div><div class="sentence"> What are we to think of time and space?</div><div class="sentence"> Time seems to have a nearer connexion with the mind, space with the body; yet time, as well as space, is necessary to our idea of either.</div><div class="sentence"> We see also that they have an analogy with one another, and that in Mathematics they often interpenetrate.</div><div class="sentence"> Space or place has been said by Kant to be the form of the outward, time of the inward sense.</div><div class="sentence"> He regards them as parts or forms of the mind.</div><div class="sentence"> But this is an unfortunate and inexpressive way of describing their relation to us.</div><div class="sentence"> For of all the phenomena present to the human mind they seem to have most the character of objective existence.</div><div class="sentence"> There is no use in asking what is beyond or behind them; we cannot get rid of them.</div><div class="sentence"> And to throw the laws of external nature which to us are the type of the immutable into the subjective side of the antithesis seems to be equally inappropriate.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">177</span> <div class="sentence">  c. When in imagination we enter into the closet of the mind and withdraw ourselves from the external world, we seem to find there more or less distinct processes which may be described by the words, 'I perceive,' 'I feel,' 'I think,' 'I want,' 'I wish,' 'I like,' 'I dislike,' 'I fear,' 'I know,' 'I remember,' 'I imagine,' 'I dream,' 'I act,' 'I endeavour,' 'I hope.'</div><div class="sentence"> These processes would seem to have the same notions attached to them in the minds of all educated persons.</div><div class="sentence"> They are distinguished from one another in thought, but they intermingle.</div><div class="sentence"> It is possible to reflect upon them or to become conscious of them in a greater or less degree, or with a greater or less continuity or attention, and thus arise the intermittent phenomena of consciousness or self-consciousness.</div><div class="sentence"> The use of all of them is possible to us at all times; and therefore in any operation of the mind the whole are latent.</div><div class="sentence"> But we are able to characterise them sufficiently by that part of the complex action which is the most prominent.</div><div class="sentence"> We have no difficulty in distinguishing an act of sight or an act of will from an act of thought, although thought is present in both of them.</div><div class="sentence"> Hence the conception of different faculties or different virtues is precarious, because each of them is passing into the other, and they are all one in the mind itself; they appear and reappear, and may all be regarded as the ever-varying phases or aspects or differences of the same mind or person.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">178</span> <div class="sentence">  d. Nearest the sense in the scale of the intellectual faculties is memory, which is a mode rather than a faculty of the mind, and accompanies all mental operations.</div><div class="sentence"> There are two principal kinds of it, recollection and recognition,—recollection in which forgotten things are recalled or return to the mind, recognition in which the mind finds itself again among things once familiar.</div><div class="sentence"> The simplest way in which we can represent the former to ourselves is by shutting our eyes and trying to recall in what we term the mind's eye the picture of the surrounding scene, or by laying down the book which we are reading and recapitulating what we can remember of it.</div><div class="sentence"> But many times more powerful than recollection is recognition, perhaps because it is more assisted by association.</div><div class="sentence"> We have known and forgotten, and after a long interval the thing which we have seen once is seen again by us, but with a different feeling, and comes back to us, not as new knowledge, but as a thing to which we ourselves impart a notion already present to us; in Plato's words, we set the stamp upon the wax.</div><div class="sentence"> Every one is aware of the difference between the first and second sight of a place, between a scene clothed with associations or bare and divested of them.</div><div class="sentence"> We say to ourselves on revisiting a spot after a long interval: How many things have happened since I last saw this!</div><div class="sentence"> There is probably no impression ever received by us of which we can venture to say that the vestiges are altogether lost, or that we might not, under some circumstances, recover it.</div><div class="sentence"> A long-forgotten knowledge may be easily renewed and therefore is very different from ignorance.</div><div class="sentence"> Of the language learnt in childhood not a word may be remembered, and yet, when a new beginning is made, the old habit soon returns, the neglected organs come back into use, and the river of speech finds out the dried-up channel.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">179</span> <div class="sentence">  e. 'Consciousness' is the most treacherous word which is employed in the study of the mind, for it is used in many senses, and has rarely, if ever, been minutely analyzed.</div><div class="sentence"> Like memory, it accompanies all mental operations, but not always continuously, and it exists in various degrees.</div><div class="sentence"> It may be imperceptible or hardly perceptible: it may be the living sense that our thoughts, actions, sufferings, are our own.</div><div class="sentence"> It is a kind of attention which we pay to ourselves, and is intermittent rather than continuous.</div><div class="sentence"> Its sphere has been exaggerated.</div><div class="sentence"> It is sometimes said to assure us of our freedom; but this is an illusion: as there may be a real freedom without consciousness of it, so there may be a consciousness of freedom without the reality.</div><div class="sentence"> It may be regarded as a higher degree of knowledge when we not only know but know that we know.</div><div class="sentence"> Consciousness is opposed to habit, inattention, sleep, death.</div><div class="sentence"> It may be illustrated by its derivative conscience, which speaks to men, not only of right and wrong in the abstract, but of right and wrong actions in reference to themselves and their circumstances.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">180</span> <div class="sentence">  f. Association is another of the ever-present phenomena of the human mind.</div><div class="sentence"> We speak of the laws of association, but this is an expression which is confusing, for the phenomenon itself is of the most capricious and uncertain sort.</div><div class="sentence"> It may be briefly described as follows.</div><div class="sentence"> The simplest case of association is that of sense.</div><div class="sentence"> When we see or hear separately one of two things, which we have previously seen or heard together, the occurrence of the one has a tendency to suggest the other.</div><div class="sentence"> So the sight or name of a house may recall to our minds the memory of those who once lived there.</div><div class="sentence"> Like may recall like and everything its opposite.</div><div class="sentence"> The parts of a whole, the terms of a series, objects lying near, words having a customary order stick together in the mind.</div><div class="sentence"> A word may bring back a passage of poetry or a whole system of philosophy; from one end of the world or from one pole of knowledge we may travel to the other in an indivisible instant.</div><div class="sentence"> The long train of association by which we pass from one point to the other, involving every sort of complex relation, so sudden, so accidental, is one of the greatest wonders of mind...This process however is not always continuous, but often intermittent: we can think of things in isolation as well as in association; we do not mean that they must all hang from one another.</div><div class="sentence"> We can begin again after an interval of rest or vacancy, as a new train of thought suddenly arises, as, for example, when we wake of a morning or after violent exercise.</div><div class="sentence"> Time, place, the same colour or sound or smell or taste, will often call up some thought or recollection either accidentally or naturally associated with them.</div><div class="sentence"> But it is equally noticeable that the new thought may occur to us, we cannot tell how or why, by the spontaneous action of the mind itself or by the latent influence of the body.</div><div class="sentence"> Both science and poetry are made up of associations or recollections, but we must observe also that the mind is not wholly dependent on them, having also the power of origination.</div></div>
