<div class="speech"><span class="ref">181</span> <div class="sentence">  There are other processes of the mind which it is good for us to study when we are at home and by ourselves,—the manner in which thought passes into act, the conflict of passion and reason in many stages, the transition from sensuality to love or sentiment and from earthly love to heavenly, the slow and silent influence of habit, which little by little changes the nature of men, the sudden change of the old nature of man into a new one, wrought by shame or by some other overwhelming impulse.</div><div class="sentence"> These are the greater phenomena of mind, and he who has thought of them for himself will live and move in a better-ordered world, and will himself be a better-ordered man.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">182</span> <div class="sentence">  At the other end of the 'globus intellectualis,' nearest, not to earth and sense, but to heaven and God, is the personality of man, by which he holds communion with the unseen world.</div><div class="sentence"> Somehow, he knows not how, somewhere, he knows not where, under this higher aspect of his being he grasps the ideas of God, freedom and immortality; he sees the forms of truth, holiness and love, and is satisfied with them.</div><div class="sentence"> No account of the mind can be complete which does not admit the reality or the possibility of another life.</div><div class="sentence"> Whether regarded as an ideal or as a fact, the highest part of man's nature and that in which it seems most nearly to approach the divine, is a phenomenon which exists, and must therefore be included within the domain of Psychology.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">183</span> <div class="sentence">  IV.</div><div class="sentence"> We admit that there is no perfect or ideal Psychology.</div><div class="sentence"> It is not a whole in the same sense in which Chemistry, Physiology, or Mathematics are wholes: that is to say, it is not a connected unity of knowledge.</div><div class="sentence"> Compared with the wealth of other sciences, it rests upon a small number of facts; and when we go beyond these, we fall into conjectures and verbal discussions.</div><div class="sentence"> The facts themselves are disjointed; the causes of them run up into other sciences, and we have no means of tracing them from one to the other.</div><div class="sentence"> Yet it may be true of this, as of other beginnings of knowledge, that the attempt to put them together has tested the truth of them, and given a stimulus to the enquiry into them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">184</span> <div class="sentence">  Psychology should be natural, not technical.</div><div class="sentence"> It should take the form which is the most intelligible to the common understanding, because it has to do with common things, which are familiar to us all.</div><div class="sentence"> It should aim at no more than every reflecting man knows or can easily verify for himself.</div><div class="sentence"> When simple and unpretentious, it is least obscured by words, least liable to fall under the influence of Physiology or Metaphysic.</div><div class="sentence"> It should argue, not from exceptional, but from ordinary phenomena.</div><div class="sentence"> It should be careful to distinguish the higher and the lower elements of human nature, and not allow one to be veiled in the disguise of the other, lest through the slippery nature of language we should pass imperceptibly from good to evil, from nature in the higher to nature in the neutral or lower sense.</div><div class="sentence"> It should assert consistently the unity of the human faculties, the unity of knowledge, the unity of God and law.</div><div class="sentence"> The difference between the will and the affections and between the reason and the passions should also be recognized by it.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">185</span> <div class="sentence">  Its sphere is supposed to be narrowed to the individual soul; but it cannot be thus separated in fact.</div><div class="sentence"> It goes back to the beginnings of things, to the first growth of language and philosophy, and to the whole science of man.</div><div class="sentence"> There can be no truth or completeness in any study of the mind which is confined to the individual.</div><div class="sentence"> The nature of language, though not the whole, is perhaps at present the most important element in our knowledge of it.</div><div class="sentence"> It is not impossible that some numerical laws may be found to have a place in the relations of mind and matter, as in the rest of nature.</div><div class="sentence"> The old Pythagorean fancy that the soul 'is or has in it harmony' may in some degree be realized.</div><div class="sentence"> But the indications of such numerical harmonies are faint; either the secret of them lies deeper than we can discover, or nature may have rebelled against the use of them in the composition of men and animals.</div><div class="sentence"> It is with qualitative rather than with quantitative differences that we are concerned in Psychology.</div><div class="sentence"> The facts relating to the mind which we obtain from Physiology are negative rather than positive.</div><div class="sentence"> They show us, not the processes of mental action, but the conditions of which when deprived the mind ceases to act.</div><div class="sentence"> It would seem as if the time had not yet arrived when we can hope to add anything of much importance to our knowledge of the mind from the investigations of the microscope.</div><div class="sentence"> The elements of Psychology can still only be learnt from reflections on ourselves, which interpret and are also interpreted by our experience of others.</div><div class="sentence"> The history of language, of philosophy, and religion, the great thoughts or inventions or discoveries which move mankind, furnish the larger moulds or outlines in which the human mind has been cast.</div><div class="sentence"> From these the individual derives so much as he is able to comprehend or has the opportunity of learning.</div></div>

</div><div id="link2H_4_0002" class="book_section">
<h2>THEAETETUS</h2>


<div class="description">
<div class="book_description">PERSONS OF THE DIALOGUE:</div> Socrates, Theodorus, Theaetetus. 
</div><div class="speech"><span class="ref">001</span> <div class="sentence">  Euclid and Terpsion meet in front of Euclid's house in Megara; they enter the house, and the dialogue is read to them by a servant.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">002</span><div class="speaker">EUCLID</div><div class="sentence">  Have you only just arrived from the country, Terpsion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">003</span><div class="speaker">TERPSION</div><div class="sentence">  No, I came some time ago:  and I have been in the Agora looking for you, and wondering that I could not find you.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">004</span><div class="speaker">EUCLID</div><div class="sentence">  But I was not in the city.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">005</span><div class="speaker">TERPSION</div><div class="sentence">  Where then?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">006</span><div class="speaker">EUCLID</div><div class="sentence">  As I was going down to the harbour, I met Theaetetus—he was being carried up to Athens from the army at Corinth.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">007</span><div class="speaker">TERPSION</div><div class="sentence">  Was he alive or dead?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">008</span><div class="speaker">EUCLID</div><div class="sentence">  He was scarcely alive, for he has been badly wounded; but he was suffering even more from the sickness which has broken out in the army.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">009</span><div class="speaker">TERPSION</div><div class="sentence">  The dysentery, you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">010</span><div class="speaker">EUCLID</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">011</span><div class="speaker">TERPSION</div><div class="sentence">  Alas!</div><div class="sentence"> what a loss he will be!</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">012</span><div class="speaker">EUCLID</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, Terpsion, he is a noble fellow; only to-day I heard some people highly praising his behaviour in this very battle.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">013</span><div class="speaker">TERPSION</div><div class="sentence">  No wonder; I should rather be surprised at hearing anything else of him.</div><div class="sentence"> But why did he go on, instead of stopping at Megara?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">014</span><div class="speaker">EUCLID</div><div class="sentence">  He wanted to get home:  although I entreated and advised him to remain, he would not listen to me; so I set him on his way, and turned back, and then I remembered what Socrates had said of him, and thought how remarkably this, like all his predictions, had been fulfilled.</div><div class="sentence"> I believe that he had seen him a little before his own death, when Theaetetus was a youth, and he had a memorable conversation with him, which he repeated to me when I came to Athens; he was full of admiration of his genius, and said that he would most certainly be a great man, if he lived.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">015</span><div class="speaker">TERPSION</div><div class="sentence">  The prophecy has certainly been fulfilled; but what was the conversation?</div><div class="sentence"> can you tell me?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">016</span><div class="speaker">EUCLID</div><div class="sentence">  No, indeed, not offhand; but I took notes of it as soon as I got home; these I filled up from memory, writing them out at leisure; and whenever I went to Athens, I asked Socrates about any point which I had forgotten, and on my return I made corrections; thus I have nearly the whole conversation written down.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">017</span><div class="speaker">TERPSION</div><div class="sentence">  I remember—you told me; and I have always been intending to ask you to show me the writing, but have put off doing so; and now, why should we not read it through?—having just come from the country, I should greatly like to rest.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">018</span><div class="speaker">EUCLID</div><div class="sentence">  I too shall be very glad of a rest, for I went with Theaetetus as far as Erineum.</div><div class="sentence"> Let us go in, then, and, while we are reposing, the servant shall read to us.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">019</span><div class="speaker">TERPSION</div><div class="sentence">  Very good.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">020</span><div class="speaker">EUCLID</div><div class="sentence">  Here is the roll, Terpsion; I may observe that I have introduced Socrates, not as narrating to me, but as actually conversing with the persons whom he mentioned—these were, Theodorus the geometrician (of Cyrene), and Theaetetus.</div><div class="sentence"> I have omitted, for the sake of convenience, the interlocutory words 'I said,' 'I remarked,' which he used when he spoke of himself, and again, 'he agreed,' or 'disagreed,' in the answer, lest the repetition of them should be troublesome.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">021</span><div class="speaker">TERPSION</div><div class="sentence">  Quite right, Euclid.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">022</span><div class="speaker">EUCLID</div><div class="sentence">  And now, boy, you may take the roll and read.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">023</span> <div class="sentence">  EUCLID'S SERVANT READS.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">024</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  If I cared enough about the Cyrenians, Theodorus, I would ask you whether there are any rising geometricians or philosophers in that part of the world.</div><div class="sentence"> But I am more interested in our own Athenian youth, and I would rather know who among them are likely to do well.</div><div class="sentence"> I observe them as far as I can myself, and I enquire of any one whom they follow, and I see that a great many of them follow you, in which they are quite right, considering your eminence in geometry and in other ways.</div><div class="sentence"> Tell me then, if you have met with any one who is good for anything.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">025</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, Socrates, I have become acquainted with one very remarkable Athenian youth, whom I commend to you as well worthy of your attention.</div><div class="sentence"> If he had been a beauty I should have been afraid to praise him, lest you should suppose that I was in love with him; but he is no beauty, and you must not be offended if I say that he is very like you; for he has a snub nose and projecting eyes, although these features are less marked in him than in you.</div><div class="sentence"> Seeing, then, that he has no personal attractions, I may freely say, that in all my acquaintance, which is very large, I never knew any one who was his equal in natural gifts:  for he has a quickness of apprehension which is almost unrivalled, and he is exceedingly gentle, and also the most courageous of men; there is a union of qualities in him such as I have never seen in any other, and should scarcely have thought possible; for those who, like him, have quick and ready and retentive wits, have generally also quick tempers; they are ships without ballast, and go darting about, and are mad rather than courageous; and the steadier sort, when they have to face study, prove stupid and cannot remember.</div><div class="sentence"> Whereas he moves surely and smoothly and successfully in the path of knowledge and enquiry; and he is full of gentleness, flowing on silently like a river of oil; at his age, it is wonderful.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">026</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That is good news; whose son is he?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">027</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  The name of his father I have forgotten, but the youth himself is the middle one of those who are approaching us; he and his companions have been anointing themselves in the outer court, and now they seem to have finished, and are coming towards us.</div><div class="sentence"> Look and see whether you know him.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">028</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I know the youth, but I do not know his name; he is the son of Euphronius the Sunian, who was himself an eminent man, and such another as his son is, according to your account of him; I believe that he left a considerable fortune.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">029</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Theaetetus, Socrates, is his name; but I rather think that the property disappeared in the hands of trustees; notwithstanding which he is wonderfully liberal.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">030</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  He must be a fine fellow; tell him to come and sit by me.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">031</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  I will.</div><div class="sentence"> Come hither, Theaetetus, and sit by Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">032</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  By all means, Theaetetus, in order that I may see the reflection of myself in your face, for Theodorus says that we are alike; and yet if each of us held in his hands a lyre, and he said that they were tuned alike, should we at once take his word, or should we ask whether he who said so was or was not a musician?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">033</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  We should ask.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">034</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And if we found that he was, we should take his word; and if not, not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">035</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">036</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And if this supposed likeness of our faces is a matter of any interest to us, we should enquire whether he who says that we are alike is a painter or not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">037</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly we should.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">038</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is Theodorus a painter?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">039</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I never heard that he was.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">040</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Is he a geometrician?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">041</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of course he is, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">042</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is he an astronomer and calculator and musician, and in general an educated man?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">043</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I think so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">044</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  If, then, he remarks on a similarity in our persons, either by way of praise or blame, there is no particular reason why we should attend to him.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">045</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should say not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">046</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But if he praises the virtue or wisdom which are the mental endowments of either of us, then he who hears the praises will naturally desire to examine him who is praised:  and he again should be willing to exhibit himself.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">047</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">048</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then now is the time, my dear Theaetetus, for me to examine, and for you to exhibit; since although Theodorus has praised many a citizen and stranger in my hearing, never did I hear him praise any one as he has been praising you.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">049</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I am glad to hear it, Socrates; but what if he was only in jest?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">050</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Nay, Theodorus is not given to jesting; and I cannot allow you to retract your consent on any such pretence as that.</div><div class="sentence"> If you do, he will have to swear to his words; and we are perfectly sure that no one will be found to impugn him.</div><div class="sentence"> Do not be shy then, but stand to your word.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">051</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I suppose I must, if you wish it.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">052</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In the first place, I should like to ask what you learn of Theodorus:  something of geometry, perhaps?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">053</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">054</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And astronomy and harmony and calculation?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">055</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I do my best.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">056</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, my boy, and so do I; and my desire is to learn of him, or of anybody who seems to understand these things.</div><div class="sentence"> And I get on pretty well in general; but there is a little difficulty which I want you and the company to aid me in investigating.</div><div class="sentence"> Will you answer me a question:  'Is not learning growing wiser about that which you learn?'</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">057</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of course.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">058</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And by wisdom the wise are wise?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">059</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">060</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is that different in any way from knowledge?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">061</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">062</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Wisdom; are not men wise in that which they know?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">063</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly they are.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">064</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then wisdom and knowledge are the same?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">065</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">066</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Herein lies the difficulty which I can never solve to my satisfaction—What is knowledge?</div><div class="sentence"> Can we answer that question?</div><div class="sentence"> What say you?</div><div class="sentence"> which of us will speak first?</div><div class="sentence"> whoever misses shall sit down, as at a game of ball, and shall be donkey, as the boys say; he who lasts out his competitors in the game without missing, shall be our king, and shall have the right of putting to us any questions which he pleases...Why is there no reply?</div><div class="sentence"> I hope, Theodorus, that I am not betrayed into rudeness by my love of conversation?</div><div class="sentence"> I only want to make us talk and be friendly and sociable.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">067</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  The reverse of rudeness, Socrates:  but I would rather that you would ask one of the young fellows; for the truth is, that I am unused to your game of question and answer, and I am too old to learn; the young will be more suitable, and they will improve more than I shall, for youth is always able to improve.</div><div class="sentence"> And so having made a beginning with Theaetetus, I would advise you to go on with him and not let him off.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">068</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Do you hear, Theaetetus, what Theodorus says?</div><div class="sentence"> The philosopher, whom you would not like to disobey, and whose word ought to be a command to a young man, bids me interrogate you.</div><div class="sentence"> Take courage, then, and nobly say what you think that knowledge is.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">069</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Well, Socrates, I will answer as you and he bid me; and if I make a mistake, you will doubtless correct me.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">070</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  We will, if we can.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">071</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Then, I think that the sciences which I learn from Theodorus—geometry, and those which you just now mentioned—are knowledge; and I would include the art of the cobbler and other craftsmen; these, each and all of, them, are knowledge.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">072</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Too much, Theaetetus, too much; the nobility and liberality of your nature make you give many and diverse things, when I am asking for one simple thing.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">073</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean, Socrates?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">074</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Perhaps nothing.</div><div class="sentence"> I will endeavour, however, to explain what I believe to be my meaning:  When you speak of cobbling, you mean the art or science of making shoes?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">075</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Just so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">076</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And when you speak of carpentering, you mean the art of making wooden implements?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">077</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I do.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">078</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In both cases you define the subject matter of each of the two arts?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">079</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">080</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But that, Theaetetus, was not the point of my question:  we wanted to know not the subjects, nor yet the number of the arts or sciences, for we were not going to count them, but we wanted to know the nature of knowledge in the abstract.</div><div class="sentence"> Am I not right?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">081</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Perfectly right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">082</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let me offer an illustration:  Suppose that a person were to ask about some very trivial and obvious thing—for example, What is clay?</div><div class="sentence"> and we were to reply, that there is a clay of potters, there is a clay of oven-makers, there is a clay of brick-makers; would not the answer be ridiculous?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">083</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Truly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">084</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In the first place, there would be an absurdity in assuming that he who asked the question would understand from our answer the nature of 'clay,' merely because we added 'of the image-makers,' or of any other workers.</div><div class="sentence"> How can a man understand the name of anything, when he does not know the nature of it?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">085</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  He cannot.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">086</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then he who does not know what science or knowledge is, has no knowledge of the art or science of making shoes?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">087</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  None.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">088</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Nor of any other science?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">089</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  No.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">090</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And when a man is asked what science or knowledge is, to give in answer the name of some art or science is ridiculous; for the question is, 'What is knowledge?'</div><div class="sentence"> and he replies, 'A knowledge of this or that.'</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">091</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">092</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Moreover, he might answer shortly and simply, but he makes an enormous circuit.</div><div class="sentence"> For example, when asked about the clay, he might have said simply, that clay is moistened earth—what sort of clay is not to the point.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">093</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, Socrates, there is no difficulty as you put the question.</div><div class="sentence"> You mean, if I am not mistaken, something like what occurred to me and to my friend here, your namesake Socrates, in a recent discussion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">094</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What was that, Theaetetus?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">095</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Theodorus was writing out for us something about roots, such as the roots of three or five, showing that they are incommensurable by the unit:  he selected other examples up to seventeen—there he stopped.</div><div class="sentence"> Now as there are innumerable roots, the notion occurred to us of attempting to include them all under one name or class.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">096</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And did you find such a class?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">097</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I think that we did; but I should like to have your opinion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">098</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let me hear.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">099</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  We divided all numbers into two classes:  those which are made up of equal factors multiplying into one another, which we compared to square figures and called square or equilateral numbers;—that was one class.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">100</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Very good.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">101</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  The intermediate numbers, such as three and five, and every other number which is made up of unequal factors, either of a greater multiplied by a less, or of a less multiplied by a greater, and when regarded as a figure, is contained in unequal sides;—all these we compared to oblong figures, and called them oblong numbers.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">102</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Capital; and what followed?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">103</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  The lines, or sides, which have for their squares the equilateral plane numbers, were called by us lengths or magnitudes; and the lines which are the roots of (or whose squares are equal to) the oblong numbers, were called powers or roots; the reason of this latter name being, that they are commensurable with the former [i.e., with the so-called lengths or magnitudes] not in linear measurement, but in the value of the superficial content of their squares; and the same about solids.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">104</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Excellent, my boys; I think that you fully justify the praises of Theodorus, and that he will not be found guilty of false witness.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">105</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  But I am unable, Socrates, to give you a similar answer about knowledge, which is what you appear to want; and therefore Theodorus is a deceiver after all.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">106</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, but if some one were to praise you for running, and to say that he never met your equal among boys, and afterwards you were beaten in a race by a grown-up man, who was a great runner—would the praise be any the less true?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">107</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">108</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is the discovery of the nature of knowledge so small a matter, as just now said?</div><div class="sentence"> Is it not one which would task the powers of men perfect in every way?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">109</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  By heaven, they should be the top of all perfection!</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">110</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, then, be of good cheer; do not say that Theodorus was mistaken about you, but do your best to ascertain the true nature of knowledge, as well as of other things.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">111</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I am eager enough, Socrates, if that would bring to light the truth.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">112</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Come, you made a good beginning just now; let your own answer about roots be your model, and as you comprehended them all in one class, try and bring the many sorts of knowledge under one definition.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">113</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I can assure you, Socrates, that I have tried very often, when the report of questions asked by you was brought to me; but I can neither persuade myself that I have a satisfactory answer to give, nor hear of any one who answers as you would have him; and I cannot shake off a feeling of anxiety.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">114</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  These are the pangs of labour, my dear Theaetetus; you have something within you which you are bringing to the birth.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">115</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I do not know, Socrates; I only say what I feel.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">116</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And have you never heard, simpleton, that I am the son of a midwife, brave and burly, whose name was Phaenarete?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">117</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, I have.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">118</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And that I myself practise midwifery?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">119</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  No, never.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">120</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let me tell you that I do though, my friend:  but you must not reveal the secret, as the world in general have not found me out; and therefore they only say of me, that I am the strangest of mortals and drive men to their wits' end.</div><div class="sentence"> Did you ever hear that too?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">121</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">122</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Shall I tell you the reason?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">123</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  By all means.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">124</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Bear in mind the whole business of the midwives, and then you will see my meaning better: —No woman, as you are probably aware, who is still able to conceive and bear, attends other women, but only those who are past bearing.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">125</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, I know.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">126</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The reason of this is said to be that Artemis—the goddess of childbirth—is not a mother, and she honours those who are like herself; but she could not allow the barren to be midwives, because human nature cannot know the mystery of an art without experience; and therefore she assigned this office to those who are too old to bear.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">127</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I dare say.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">128</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And I dare say too, or rather I am absolutely certain, that the midwives know better than others who is pregnant and who is not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">129</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">130</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And by the use of potions and incantations they are able to arouse the pangs and to soothe them at will; they can make those bear who have a difficulty in bearing, and if they think fit they can smother the embryo in the womb.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">131</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  They can.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">132</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Did you ever remark that they are also most cunning matchmakers, and have a thorough knowledge of what unions are likely to produce a brave brood?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">133</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  No, never.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">134</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then let me tell you that this is their greatest pride, more than cutting the umbilical cord.</div><div class="sentence"> And if you reflect, you will see that the same art which cultivates and gathers in the fruits of the earth, will be most likely to know in what soils the several plants or seeds should be deposited.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">135</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, the same art.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">136</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And do you suppose that with women the case is otherwise?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">137</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should think not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">138</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not; but midwives are respectable women who have a character to lose, and they avoid this department of their profession, because they are afraid of being called procuresses, which is a name given to those who join together man and woman in an unlawful and unscientific way; and yet the true midwife is also the true and only matchmaker.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">139</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">140</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Such are the midwives, whose task is a very important one, but not so important as mine; for women do not bring into the world at one time real children, and at another time counterfeits which are with difficulty distinguished from them; if they did, then the discernment of the true and false birth would be the crowning achievement of the art of midwifery—you would think so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">141</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Indeed I should.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">142</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, my art of midwifery is in most respects like theirs; but differs, in that I attend men and not women; and look after their souls when they are in labour, and not after their bodies:  and the triumph of my art is in thoroughly examining whether the thought which the mind of the young man brings forth is a false idol or a noble and true birth.</div><div class="sentence"> And like the midwives, I am barren, and the reproach which is often made against me, that I ask questions of others and have not the wit to answer them myself, is very just—the reason is, that the god compels me to be a midwife, but does not allow me to bring forth.</div><div class="sentence"> And therefore I am not myself at all wise, nor have I anything to show which is the invention or birth of my own soul, but those who converse with me profit.</div><div class="sentence"> Some of them appear dull enough at first, but afterwards, as our acquaintance ripens, if the god is gracious to them, they all make astonishing progress; and this in the opinion of others as well as in their own.</div><div class="sentence"> It is quite clear that they never learned anything from me; the many fine discoveries to which they cling are of their own making.</div><div class="sentence"> But to me and the god they owe their delivery.</div><div class="sentence"> And the proof of my words is, that many of them in their ignorance, either in their self-conceit despising me, or falling under the influence of others, have gone away too soon; and have not only lost the children of whom I had previously delivered them by an ill bringing up, but have stifled whatever else they had in them by evil communications, being fonder of lies and shams than of the truth; and they have at last ended by seeing themselves, as others see them, to be great fools.</div><div class="sentence"> Aristeides, the son of Lysimachus, is one of them, and there are many others.</div><div class="sentence"> The truants often return to me, and beg that I would consort with them again—they are ready to go to me on their knees—and then, if my familiar allows, which is not always the case, I receive them, and they begin to grow again.</div><div class="sentence"> Dire are the pangs which my art is able to arouse and to allay in those who consort with me, just like the pangs of women in childbirth; night and day they are full of perplexity and travail which is even worse than that of the women.</div><div class="sentence"> So much for them.</div><div class="sentence"> And there are others, Theaetetus, who come to me apparently having nothing in them; and as I know that they have no need of my art, I coax them into marrying some one, and by the grace of God I can generally tell who is likely to do them good.</div><div class="sentence"> Many of them I have given away to Prodicus, and many to other inspired sages.</div><div class="sentence"> I tell you this long story, friend Theaetetus, because I suspect, as indeed you seem to think yourself, that you are in labour—great with some conception.</div><div class="sentence"> Come then to me, who am a midwife's son and myself a midwife, and do your best to answer the questions which I will ask you.</div><div class="sentence"> And if I abstract and expose your first-born, because I discover upon inspection that the conception which you have formed is a vain shadow, do not quarrel with me on that account, as the manner of women is when their first children are taken from them.</div><div class="sentence"> For I have actually known some who were ready to bite me when I deprived them of a darling folly; they did not perceive that I acted from goodwill, not knowing that no god is the enemy of man—that was not within the range of their ideas; neither am I their enemy in all this, but it would be wrong for me to admit falsehood, or to stifle the truth.</div><div class="sentence"> Once more, then, Theaetetus, I repeat my old question, 'What is knowledge?</div><div class="sentence"> '—and do not say that you cannot tell; but quit yourself like a man, and by the help of God you will be able to tell.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">143</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  At any rate, Socrates, after such an exhortation I should be ashamed of not trying to do my best.</div><div class="sentence"> Now he who knows perceives what he knows, and, as far as I can see at present, knowledge is perception.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">144</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Bravely said, boy; that is the way in which you should express your opinion.</div><div class="sentence"> And now, let us examine together this conception of yours, and see whether it is a true birth or a mere wind-egg: —You say that knowledge is perception?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">145</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">146</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, you have delivered yourself of a very important doctrine about knowledge; it is indeed the opinion of Protagoras, who has another way of expressing it.</div><div class="sentence"> Man, he says, is the measure of all things, of the existence of things that are, and of the non-existence of things that are not: —You have read him?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">147</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  O yes, again and again.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">148</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Does he not say that things are to you such as they appear to you, and to me such as they appear to me, and that you and I are men?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">149</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, he says so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">150</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  A wise man is not likely to talk nonsense.</div><div class="sentence"> Let us try to understand him:  the same wind is blowing, and yet one of us may be cold and the other not, or one may be slightly and the other very cold?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">151</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Quite true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">152</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Now is the wind, regarded not in relation to us but absolutely, cold or not; or are we to say, with Protagoras, that the wind is cold to him who is cold, and not to him who is not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">153</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I suppose the last.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">154</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then it must appear so to each of them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">155</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">156</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And 'appears to him' means the same as 'he perceives.'</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">157</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">158</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then appearing and perceiving coincide in the case of hot and cold, and in similar instances; for things appear, or may be supposed to be, to each one such as he perceives them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">159</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">160</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then perception is always of existence, and being the same as knowledge is unerring?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">161</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">162</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In the name of the Graces, what an almighty wise man Protagoras must have been!</div><div class="sentence"> He spoke these things in a parable to the common herd, like you and me, but told the truth, 'his Truth,' (In allusion to a book of Protagoras' which bore this title.)</div><div class="sentence"> in secret to his own disciples.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">163</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean, Socrates?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">164</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I am about to speak of a high argument, in which all things are said to be relative; you cannot rightly call anything by any name, such as great or small, heavy or light, for the great will be small and the heavy light—there is no single thing or quality, but out of motion and change and admixture all things are becoming relatively to one another, which 'becoming' is by us incorrectly called being, but is really becoming, for nothing ever is, but all things are becoming.</div><div class="sentence"> Summon all philosophers—Protagoras, Heracleitus, Empedocles, and the rest of them, one after another, and with the exception of Parmenides they will agree with you in this.</div><div class="sentence"> Summon the great masters of either kind of poetry—Epicharmus, the prince of Comedy, and Homer of Tragedy; when the latter sings of</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">165</span> <div class="sentence">  'Ocean whence sprang the gods, and mother Tethys,'</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">166</span> <div class="sentence">  does he not mean that all things are the offspring, of flux and motion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">167</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I think so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">168</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And who could take up arms against such a great army having Homer for its general, and not appear ridiculous?</div><div class="sentence"> (Compare Cratylus.)</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">169</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Who indeed, Socrates?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">170</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, Theaetetus; and there are plenty of other proofs which will show that motion is the source of what is called being and becoming, and inactivity of not-being and destruction; for fire and warmth, which are supposed to be the parent and guardian of all other things, are born of movement and of friction, which is a kind of motion;—is not this the origin of fire?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">171</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  It is.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">172</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the race of animals is generated in the same way?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">173</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">174</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is not the bodily habit spoiled by rest and idleness, but preserved for a long time by motion and exercise?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">175</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">176</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And what of the mental habit?</div><div class="sentence"> Is not the soul informed, and improved, and preserved by study and attention, which are motions; but when at rest, which in the soul only means want of attention and study, is uninformed, and speedily forgets whatever she has learned?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">177</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">178</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then motion is a good, and rest an evil, to the soul as well as to the body?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">179</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">180</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I may add, that breathless calm, stillness and the like waste and impair, while wind and storm preserve; and the palmary argument of all, which I strongly urge, is the golden chain in Homer, by which he means the sun, thereby indicating that so long as the sun and the heavens go round in their orbits, all things human and divine are and are preserved, but if they were chained up and their motions ceased, then all things would be destroyed, and, as the saying is, turned upside down.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">181</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I believe, Socrates, that you have truly explained his meaning.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">182</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then now apply his doctrine to perception, my good friend, and first of all to vision; that which you call white colour is not in your eyes, and is not a distinct thing which exists out of them.</div><div class="sentence"> And you must not assign any place to it:  for if it had position it would be, and be at rest, and there would be no process of becoming.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">183</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Then what is colour?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">184</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let us carry the principle which has just been affirmed, that nothing is self-existent, and then we shall see that white, black, and every other colour, arises out of the eye meeting the appropriate motion, and that what we call a colour is in each case neither the active nor the passive element, but something which passes between them, and is peculiar to each percipient; are you quite certain that the several colours appear to a dog or to any animal whatever as they appear to you?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">185</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Far from it.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">186</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Or that anything appears the same to you as to another man?</div><div class="sentence"> Are you so profoundly convinced of this?</div><div class="sentence"> Rather would it not be true that it never appears exactly the same to you, because you are never exactly the same?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">187</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  The latter.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">188</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And if that with which I compare myself in size, or which I apprehend by touch, were great or white or hot, it could not become different by mere contact with another unless it actually changed; nor again, if the comparing or apprehending subject were great or white or hot, could this, when unchanged from within, become changed by any approximation or affection of any other thing.</div><div class="sentence"> The fact is that in our ordinary way of speaking we allow ourselves to be driven into most ridiculous and wonderful contradictions, as Protagoras and all who take his line of argument would remark.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">189</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  How?</div><div class="sentence"> and of what sort do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">190</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  A little instance will sufficiently explain my meaning:  Here are six dice, which are more by a half when compared with four, and fewer by a half than twelve—they are more and also fewer.</div><div class="sentence"> How can you or any one maintain the contrary?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">191</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">192</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, then, suppose that Protagoras or some one asks whether anything can become greater or more if not by increasing, how would you answer him, Theaetetus?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">193</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should say 'No,' Socrates, if I were to speak my mind in reference to this last question, and if I were not afraid of contradicting my former answer.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">194</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Capital!</div><div class="sentence"> excellent!</div><div class="sentence"> spoken like an oracle, my boy!</div><div class="sentence"> And if you reply 'Yes,' there will be a case for Euripides; for our tongue will be unconvinced, but not our mind.</div><div class="sentence"> (In allusion to the well-known line of Euripides, Hippol.</div><div class="sentence"> :  e gloss omomoch e de thren anomotos.)</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">195</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">196</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The thoroughbred Sophists, who know all that can be known about the mind, and argue only out of the superfluity of their wits, would have had a regular sparring-match over this, and would have knocked their arguments together finely.</div><div class="sentence"> But you and I, who have no professional aims, only desire to see what is the mutual relation of these principles,—whether they are consistent with each or not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">197</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, that would be my desire.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">198</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And mine too.</div><div class="sentence"> But since this is our feeling, and there is plenty of time, why should we not calmly and patiently review our own thoughts, and thoroughly examine and see what these appearances in us really are?</div><div class="sentence"> If I am not mistaken, they will be described by us as follows: —first, that nothing can become greater or less, either in number or magnitude, while remaining equal to itself—you would agree?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">199</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">200</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Secondly, that without addition or subtraction there is no increase or diminution of anything, but only equality.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">201</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Quite true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">202</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Thirdly, that what was not before cannot be afterwards, without becoming and having become.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">203</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, truly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">204</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  These three axioms, if I am not mistaken, are fighting with one another in our minds in the case of the dice, or, again, in such a case as this—if I were to say that I, who am of a certain height and taller than you, may within a year, without gaining or losing in height, be not so tall—not that I should have lost, but that you would have increased.</div><div class="sentence"> In such a case, I am afterwards what I once was not, and yet I have not become; for I could not have become without becoming, neither could I have become less without losing somewhat of my height; and I could give you ten thousand examples of similar contradictions, if we admit them at all.</div><div class="sentence"> I believe that you follow me, Theaetetus; for I suspect that you have thought of these questions before now.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">205</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, Socrates, and I am amazed when I think of them; by the Gods I am!</div><div class="sentence"> and I want to know what on earth they mean; and there are times when my head quite swims with the contemplation of them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">206</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I see, my dear Theaetetus, that Theodorus had a true insight into your nature when he said that you were a philosopher, for wonder is the feeling of a philosopher, and philosophy begins in wonder.</div><div class="sentence"> He was not a bad genealogist who said that Iris (the messenger of heaven) is the child of Thaumas (wonder).</div><div class="sentence"> But do you begin to see what is the explanation of this perplexity on the hypothesis which we attribute to Protagoras?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">207</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Not as yet.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">208</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then you will be obliged to me if I help you to unearth the hidden 'truth' of a famous man or school.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">209</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  To be sure, I shall be very much obliged.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">210</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Take a look round, then, and see that none of the uninitiated are listening.</div><div class="sentence"> Now by the uninitiated I mean the people who believe in nothing but what they can grasp in their hands, and who will not allow that action or generation or anything invisible can have real existence.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">211</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, indeed, Socrates, they are very hard and impenetrable mortals.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">212</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, my boy, outer barbarians.</div><div class="sentence"> Far more ingenious are the brethren whose mysteries I am about to reveal to you.</div><div class="sentence"> Their first principle is, that all is motion, and upon this all the affections of which we were just now speaking are supposed to depend:  there is nothing but motion, which has two forms, one active and the other passive, both in endless number; and out of the union and friction of them there is generated a progeny endless in number, having two forms, sense and the object of sense, which are ever breaking forth and coming to the birth at the same moment.</div><div class="sentence"> The senses are variously named hearing, seeing, smelling; there is the sense of heat, cold, pleasure, pain, desire, fear, and many more which have names, as well as innumerable others which are without them; each has its kindred object,—each variety of colour has a corresponding variety of sight, and so with sound and hearing, and with the rest of the senses and the objects akin to them.</div><div class="sentence"> Do you see, Theaetetus, the bearings of this tale on the preceding argument?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">213</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Indeed I do not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">214</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then attend, and I will try to finish the story.</div><div class="sentence"> The purport is that all these things are in motion, as I was saying, and that this motion is of two kinds, a slower and a quicker; and the slower elements have their motions in the same place and with reference to things near them, and so they beget; but what is begotten is swifter, for it is carried to fro, and moves from place to place.</div><div class="sentence"> Apply this to sense: —When the eye and the appropriate object meet together and give birth to whiteness and the sensation connatural with it, which could not have been given by either of them going elsewhere, then, while the sight is flowing from the eye, whiteness proceeds from the object which combines in producing the colour; and so the eye is fulfilled with sight, and really sees, and becomes, not sight, but a seeing eye; and the object which combined to form the colour is fulfilled with whiteness, and becomes not whiteness but a white thing, whether wood or stone or whatever the object may be which happens to be coloured white.</div><div class="sentence"> And this is true of all sensible objects, hard, warm, and the like, which are similarly to be regarded, as I was saying before, not as having any absolute existence, but as being all of them of whatever kind generated by motion in their intercourse with one another; for of the agent and patient, as existing in separation, no trustworthy conception, as they say, can be formed, for the agent has no existence until united with the patient, and the patient has no existence until united with the agent; and that which by uniting with something becomes an agent, by meeting with some other thing is converted into a patient.</div><div class="sentence"> And from all these considerations, as I said at first, there arises a general reflection, that there is no one self-existent thing, but everything is becoming and in relation; and being must be altogether abolished, although from habit and ignorance we are compelled even in this discussion to retain the use of the term.</div><div class="sentence"> But great philosophers tell us that we are not to allow either the word 'something,' or 'belonging to something,' or 'to me,' or 'this,' or 'that,' or any other detaining name to be used, in the language of nature all things are being created and destroyed, coming into being and passing into new forms; nor can any name fix or detain them; he who attempts to fix them is easily refuted.</div><div class="sentence"> And this should be the way of speaking, not only of particulars but of aggregates; such aggregates as are expressed in the word 'man,' or 'stone,' or any name of an animal or of a class.</div><div class="sentence"> O Theaetetus, are not these speculations sweet as honey?</div><div class="sentence"> And do you not like the taste of them in the mouth?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">215</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I do not know what to say, Socrates; for, indeed, I cannot make out whether you are giving your own opinion or only wanting to draw me out.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">216</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You forget, my friend, that I neither know, nor profess to know, anything of these matters; you are the person who is in labour, I am the barren midwife; and this is why I soothe you, and offer you one good thing after another, that you may taste them.</div><div class="sentence"> And I hope that I may at last help to bring your own opinion into the light of day:  when this has been accomplished, then we will determine whether what you have brought forth is only a wind-egg or a real and genuine birth.</div><div class="sentence"> Therefore, keep up your spirits, and answer like a man what you think.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">217</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Ask me.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">218</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then once more:  Is it your opinion that nothing is but what becomes?—the good and the noble, as well as all the other things which we were just now mentioning?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">219</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  When I hear you discoursing in this style, I think that there is a great deal in what you say, and I am very ready to assent.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">220</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let us not leave the argument unfinished, then; for there still remains to be considered an objection which may be raised about dreams and diseases, in particular about madness, and the various illusions of hearing and sight, or of other senses.</div><div class="sentence"> For you know that in all these cases the esse-percipi theory appears to be unmistakably refuted, since in dreams and illusions we certainly have false perceptions; and far from saying that everything is which appears, we should rather say that nothing is which appears.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">221</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">222</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But then, my boy, how can any one contend that knowledge is perception, or that to every man what appears is?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">223</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I am afraid to say, Socrates, that I have nothing to answer, because you rebuked me just now for making this excuse; but I certainly cannot undertake to argue that madmen or dreamers think truly, when they imagine, some of them that they are gods, and others that they can fly, and are flying in their sleep.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">224</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Do you see another question which can be raised about these phenomena, notably about dreaming and waking?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">225</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What question?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">226</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  A question which I think that you must often have heard persons ask: —How can you determine whether at this moment we are sleeping, and all our thoughts are a dream; or whether we are awake, and talking to one another in the waking state?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">227</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Indeed, Socrates, I do not know how to prove the one any more than the other, for in both cases the facts precisely correspond;—and there is no difficulty in supposing that during all this discussion we have been talking to one another in a dream; and when in a dream we seem to be narrating dreams, the resemblance of the two states is quite astonishing.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">228</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You see, then, that a doubt about the reality of sense is easily raised, since there may even be a doubt whether we are awake or in a dream.</div><div class="sentence"> And as our time is equally divided between sleeping and waking, in either sphere of existence the soul contends that the thoughts which are present to our minds at the time are true; and during one half of our lives we affirm the truth of the one, and, during the other half, of the other; and are equally confident of both.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">229</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Most true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">230</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And may not the same be said of madness and other disorders?</div><div class="sentence"> the difference is only that the times are not equal.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">231</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">232</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is truth or falsehood to be determined by duration of time?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">233</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  That would be in many ways ridiculous.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">234</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But can you certainly determine by any other means which of these opinions is true?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">235</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I do not think that I can.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">236</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Listen, then, to a statement of the other side of the argument, which is made by the champions of appearance.</div><div class="sentence"> They would say, as I imagine—Can that which is wholly other than something, have the same quality as that from which it differs?</div><div class="sentence"> and observe, Theaetetus, that the word 'other' means not 'partially,' but 'wholly other.'</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">237</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly, putting the question as you do, that which is wholly other cannot either potentially or in any other way be the same.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">238</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And must therefore be admitted to be unlike?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">239</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">240</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  If, then, anything happens to become like or unlike itself or another, when it becomes like we call it the same—when unlike, other?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">241</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">242</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Were we not saying that there are agents many and infinite, and patients many and infinite?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">243</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">244</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And also that different combinations will produce results which are not the same, but different?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">245</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">246</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let us take you and me, or anything as an example: —There is Socrates in health, and Socrates sick—Are they like or unlike?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">247</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  You mean to compare Socrates in health as a whole, and Socrates in sickness as a whole?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">248</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Exactly; that is my meaning.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">249</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I answer, they are unlike.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">250</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And if unlike, they are other?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">251</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">252</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And would you not say the same of Socrates sleeping and waking, or in any of the states which we were mentioning?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">253</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">254</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  All agents have a different patient in Socrates, accordingly as he is well or ill.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">255</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of course.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">256</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And I who am the patient, and that which is the agent, will produce something different in each of the two cases?</div></div>
