<div class="speech"><span class="ref">257</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">258</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The wine which I drink when I am in health, appears sweet and pleasant to me?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">259</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">260</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  For, as has been already acknowledged, the patient and agent meet together and produce sweetness and a perception of sweetness, which are in simultaneous motion, and the perception which comes from the patient makes the tongue percipient, and the quality of sweetness which arises out of and is moving about the wine, makes the wine both to be and to appear sweet to the healthy tongue.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">261</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly; that has been already acknowledged.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">262</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But when I am sick, the wine really acts upon another and a different person?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">263</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">264</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The combination of the draught of wine, and the Socrates who is sick, produces quite another result; which is the sensation of bitterness in the tongue, and the motion and creation of bitterness in and about the wine, which becomes not bitterness but something bitter; as I myself become not perception but percipient?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">265</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">266</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  There is no other object of which I shall ever have the same perception, for another object would give another perception, and would make the percipient other and different; nor can that object which affects me, meeting another subject, produce the same, or become similar, for that too would produce another result from another subject, and become different.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">267</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">268</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Neither can I by myself, have this sensation, nor the object by itself, this quality.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">269</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">270</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  When I perceive I must become percipient of something—there can be no such thing as perceiving and perceiving nothing; the object, whether it become sweet, bitter, or of any other quality, must have relation to a percipient; nothing can become sweet which is sweet to no one.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">271</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">272</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then the inference is, that we (the agent and patient) are or become in relation to one another; there is a law which binds us one to the other, but not to any other existence, nor each of us to himself; and therefore we can only be bound to one another; so that whether a person says that a thing is or becomes, he must say that it is or becomes to or of or in relation to something else; but he must not say or allow any one else to say that anything is or becomes absolutely: —such is our conclusion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">273</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">274</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then, if that which acts upon me has relation to me and to no other, I and no other am the percipient of it?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">275</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of course.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">276</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then my perception is true to me, being inseparable from my own being; and, as Protagoras says, to myself I am judge of what is and what is not to me.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">277</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I suppose so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">278</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  How then, if I never err, and if my mind never trips in the conception of being or becoming, can I fail of knowing that which I perceive?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">279</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  You cannot.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">280</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then you were quite right in affirming that knowledge is only perception; and the meaning turns out to be the same, whether with Homer and Heracleitus, and all that company, you say that all is motion and flux, or with the great sage Protagoras, that man is the measure of all things; or with Theaetetus, that, given these premises, perception is knowledge.</div><div class="sentence"> Am I not right, Theaetetus, and is not this your new-born child, of which I have delivered you?</div><div class="sentence"> What say you?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">281</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I cannot but agree, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">282</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then this is the child, however he may turn out, which you and I have with difficulty brought into the world.</div><div class="sentence"> And now that he is born, we must run round the hearth with him, and see whether he is worth rearing, or is only a wind-egg and a sham.</div><div class="sentence"> Is he to be reared in any case, and not exposed?</div><div class="sentence"> or will you bear to see him rejected, and not get into a passion if I take away your first-born?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">283</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Theaetetus will not be angry, for he is very good-natured.</div><div class="sentence"> But tell me, Socrates, in heaven's name, is this, after all, not the truth?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">284</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You, Theodorus, are a lover of theories, and now you innocently fancy that I am a bag full of them, and can easily pull one out which will overthrow its predecessor.</div><div class="sentence"> But you do not see that in reality none of these theories come from me; they all come from him who talks with me.</div><div class="sentence"> I only know just enough to extract them from the wisdom of another, and to receive them in a spirit of fairness.</div><div class="sentence"> And now I shall say nothing myself, but shall endeavour to elicit something from our young friend.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">285</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Do as you say, Socrates; you are quite right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">286</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Shall I tell you, Theodorus, what amazes me in your acquaintance Protagoras?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">287</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  What is it?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">288</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I am charmed with his doctrine, that what appears is to each one, but I wonder that he did not begin his book on Truth with a declaration that a pig or a dog-faced baboon, or some other yet stranger monster which has sensation, is the measure of all things; then he might have shown a magnificent contempt for our opinion of him by informing us at the outset that while we were reverencing him like a God for his wisdom he was no better than a tadpole, not to speak of his fellow-men—would not this have produced an overpowering effect?</div><div class="sentence"> For if truth is only sensation, and no man can discern another's feelings better than he, or has any superior right to determine whether his opinion is true or false, but each, as we have several times repeated, is to himself the sole judge, and everything that he judges is true and right, why, my friend, should Protagoras be preferred to the place of wisdom and instruction, and deserve to be well paid, and we poor ignoramuses have to go to him, if each one is the measure of his own wisdom?</div><div class="sentence"> Must he not be talking 'ad captandum' in all this?</div><div class="sentence"> I say nothing of the ridiculous predicament in which my own midwifery and the whole art of dialectic is placed; for the attempt to supervise or refute the notions or opinions of others would be a tedious and enormous piece of folly, if to each man his own are right; and this must be the case if Protagoras' Truth is the real truth, and the philosopher is not merely amusing himself by giving oracles out of the shrine of his book.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">289</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  He was a friend of mine, Socrates, as you were saying, and therefore I cannot have him refuted by my lips, nor can I oppose you when I agree with you; please, then, to take Theaetetus again; he seemed to answer very nicely.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">290</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  If you were to go into a Lacedaemonian palestra, Theodorus, would you have a right to look on at the naked wrestlers, some of them making a poor figure, if you did not strip and give them an opportunity of judging of your own person?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">291</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Why not, Socrates, if they would allow me, as I think you will, in consideration of my age and stiffness; let some more supple youth try a fall with you, and do not drag me into the gymnasium.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">292</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Your will is my will, Theodorus, as the proverbial philosophers say, and therefore I will return to the sage Theaetetus:  Tell me, Theaetetus, in reference to what I was saying, are you not lost in wonder, like myself, when you find that all of a sudden you are raised to the level of the wisest of men, or indeed of the gods?—for you would assume the measure of Protagoras to apply to the gods as well as men?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">293</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly I should, and I confess to you that I am lost in wonder.</div><div class="sentence"> At first hearing, I was quite satisfied with the doctrine, that whatever appears is to each one, but now the face of things has changed.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">294</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Why, my dear boy, you are young, and therefore your ear is quickly caught and your mind influenced by popular arguments.</div><div class="sentence"> Protagoras, or some one speaking on his behalf, will doubtless say in reply,—Good people, young and old, you meet and harangue, and bring in the gods, whose existence or non-existence I banish from writing and speech, or you talk about the reason of man being degraded to the level of the brutes, which is a telling argument with the multitude, but not one word of proof or demonstration do you offer.</div><div class="sentence"> All is probability with you, and yet surely you and Theodorus had better reflect whether you are disposed to admit of probability and figures of speech in matters of such importance.</div><div class="sentence"> He or any other mathematician who argued from probabilities and likelihoods in geometry, would not be worth an ace.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">295</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  But neither you nor we, Socrates, would be satisfied with such arguments.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">296</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then you and Theodorus mean to say that we must look at the matter in some other way?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">297</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, in quite another way.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">298</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the way will be to ask whether perception is or is not the same as knowledge; for this was the real point of our argument, and with a view to this we raised (did we not?)</div><div class="sentence"> those many strange questions.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">299</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">300</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Shall we say that we know every thing which we see and hear?</div><div class="sentence"> for example, shall we say that not having learned, we do not hear the language of foreigners when they speak to us?</div><div class="sentence"> or shall we say that we not only hear, but know what they are saying?</div><div class="sentence"> Or again, if we see letters which we do not understand, shall we say that we do not see them?</div><div class="sentence"> or shall we aver that, seeing them, we must know them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">301</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  We shall say, Socrates, that we know what we actually see and hear of them—that is to say, we see and know the figure and colour of the letters, and we hear and know the elevation or depression of the sound of them; but we do not perceive by sight and hearing, or know, that which grammarians and interpreters teach about them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">302</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Capital, Theaetetus; and about this there shall be no dispute, because I want you to grow; but there is another difficulty coming, which you will also have to repulse.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">303</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What is it?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">304</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Some one will say, Can a man who has ever known anything, and still has and preserves a memory of that which he knows, not know that which he remembers at the time when he remembers?</div><div class="sentence"> I have, I fear, a tedious way of putting a simple question, which is only, whether a man who has learned, and remembers, can fail to know?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">305</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Impossible, Socrates; the supposition is monstrous.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">306</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Am I talking nonsense, then?</div><div class="sentence"> Think:  is not seeing perceiving, and is not sight perception?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">307</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">308</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And if our recent definition holds, every man knows that which he has seen?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">309</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">310</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And you would admit that there is such a thing as memory?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">311</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">312</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is memory of something or of nothing?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">313</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of something, surely.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">314</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Of things learned and perceived, that is?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">315</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">316</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Often a man remembers that which he has seen?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">317</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">318</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And if he closed his eyes, would he forget?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">319</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Who, Socrates, would dare to say so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">320</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But we must say so, if the previous argument is to be maintained.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">321</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean?</div><div class="sentence"> I am not quite sure that I understand you, though I have a strong suspicion that you are right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">322</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  As thus:  he who sees knows, as we say, that which he sees; for perception and sight and knowledge are admitted to be the same.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">323</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">324</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But he who saw, and has knowledge of that which he saw, remembers, when he closes his eyes, that which he no longer sees.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">325</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">326</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And seeing is knowing, and therefore not-seeing is not-knowing?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">327</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">328</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then the inference is, that a man may have attained the knowledge of something, which he may remember and yet not know, because he does not see; and this has been affirmed by us to be a monstrous supposition.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">329</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Most true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">330</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Thus, then, the assertion that knowledge and perception are one, involves a manifest impossibility?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">331</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">332</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then they must be distinguished?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">333</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I suppose that they must.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">334</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Once more we shall have to begin, and ask 'What is knowledge?'</div><div class="sentence"> and yet, Theaetetus, what are we going to do?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">335</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  About what?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">336</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Like a good-for-nothing cock, without having won the victory, we walk away from the argument and crow.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">337</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  How do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">338</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  After the manner of disputers (Lys.</div><div class="sentence"> ; Phaedo; Republic), we were satisfied with mere verbal consistency, and were well pleased if in this way we could gain an advantage.</div><div class="sentence"> Although professing not to be mere Eristics, but philosophers, I suspect that we have unconsciously fallen into the error of that ingenious class of persons.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">339</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I do not as yet understand you.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">340</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then I will try to explain myself:  just now we asked the question, whether a man who had learned and remembered could fail to know, and we showed that a person who had seen might remember when he had his eyes shut and could not see, and then he would at the same time remember and not know.</div><div class="sentence"> But this was an impossibility.</div><div class="sentence"> And so the Protagorean fable came to nought, and yours also, who maintained that knowledge is the same as perception.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">341</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">342</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And yet, my friend, I rather suspect that the result would have been different if Protagoras, who was the father of the first of the two brats, had been alive; he would have had a great deal to say on their behalf.</div><div class="sentence"> But he is dead, and we insult over his orphan child; and even the guardians whom he left, and of whom our friend Theodorus is one, are unwilling to give any help, and therefore I suppose that I must take up his cause myself, and see justice done?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">343</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Not I, Socrates, but rather Callias, the son of Hipponicus, is guardian of his orphans.</div><div class="sentence"> I was too soon diverted from the abstractions of dialectic to geometry.</div><div class="sentence"> Nevertheless, I shall be grateful to you if you assist him.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">344</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Very good, Theodorus; you shall see how I will come to the rescue.</div><div class="sentence"> If a person does not attend to the meaning of terms as they are commonly used in argument, he may be involved even in greater paradoxes than these.</div><div class="sentence"> Shall I explain this matter to you or to Theaetetus?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">345</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  To both of us, and let the younger answer; he will incur less disgrace if he is discomfited.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">346</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then now let me ask the awful question, which is this: —Can a man know and also not know that which he knows?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">347</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  How shall we answer, Theaetetus?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">348</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  He cannot, I should say.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">349</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  He can, if you maintain that seeing is knowing.</div><div class="sentence"> When you are imprisoned in a well, as the saying is, and the self-assured adversary closes one of your eyes with his hand, and asks whether you can see his cloak with the eye which he has closed, how will you answer the inevitable man?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">350</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should answer, 'Not with that eye but with the other.'</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">351</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then you see and do not see the same thing at the same time.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">352</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, in a certain sense.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">353</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  None of that, he will reply; I do not ask or bid you answer in what sense you know, but only whether you know that which you do not know.</div><div class="sentence"> You have been proved to see that which you do not see; and you have already admitted that seeing is knowing, and that not-seeing is not-knowing:  I leave you to draw the inference.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">354</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes; the inference is the contradictory of my assertion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">355</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, my marvel, and there might have been yet worse things in store for you, if an opponent had gone on to ask whether you can have a sharp and also a dull knowledge, and whether you can know near, but not at a distance, or know the same thing with more or less intensity, and so on without end.</div><div class="sentence"> Such questions might have been put to you by a light-armed mercenary, who argued for pay.</div><div class="sentence"> He would have lain in wait for you, and when you took up the position, that sense is knowledge, he would have made an assault upon hearing, smelling, and the other senses;—he would have shown you no mercy; and while you were lost in envy and admiration of his wisdom, he would have got you into his net, out of which you would not have escaped until you had come to an understanding about the sum to be paid for your release.</div><div class="sentence"> Well, you ask, and how will Protagoras reinforce his position?</div><div class="sentence"> Shall I answer for him?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">356</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  By all means.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">357</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  He will repeat all those things which we have been urging on his behalf, and then he will close with us in disdain, and say: —The worthy Socrates asked a little boy, whether the same man could remember and not know the same thing, and the boy said No, because he was frightened, and could not see what was coming, and then Socrates made fun of poor me.</div><div class="sentence"> The truth is, O slatternly Socrates, that when you ask questions about any assertion of mine, and the person asked is found tripping, if he has answered as I should have answered, then I am refuted, but if he answers something else, then he is refuted and not I.</div><div class="sentence"> For do you really suppose that any one would admit the memory which a man has of an impression which has passed away to be the same with that which he experienced at the time?</div><div class="sentence"> Assuredly not.</div><div class="sentence"> Or would he hesitate to acknowledge that the same man may know and not know the same thing?</div><div class="sentence"> Or, if he is afraid of making this admission, would he ever grant that one who has become unlike is the same as before he became unlike?</div><div class="sentence"> Or would he admit that a man is one at all, and not rather many and infinite as the changes which take place in him?</div><div class="sentence"> I speak by the card in order to avoid entanglements of words.</div><div class="sentence"> But, O my good sir, he will say, come to the argument in a more generous spirit; and either show, if you can, that our sensations are not relative and individual, or, if you admit them to be so, prove that this does not involve the consequence that the appearance becomes, or, if you will have the word, is, to the individual only.</div><div class="sentence"> As to your talk about pigs and baboons, you are yourself behaving like a pig, and you teach your hearers to make sport of my writings in the same ignorant manner; but this is not to your credit.</div><div class="sentence"> For I declare that the truth is as I have written, and that each of us is a measure of existence and of non-existence.</div><div class="sentence"> Yet one man may be a thousand times better than another in proportion as different things are and appear to him.</div><div class="sentence"> And I am far from saying that wisdom and the wise man have no existence; but I say that the wise man is he who makes the evils which appear and are to a man, into goods which are and appear to him.</div><div class="sentence"> And I would beg you not to press my words in the letter, but to take the meaning of them as I will explain them.</div><div class="sentence"> Remember what has been already said,—that to the sick man his food appears to be and is bitter, and to the man in health the opposite of bitter.</div><div class="sentence"> Now I cannot conceive that one of these men can be or ought to be made wiser than the other:  nor can you assert that the sick man because he has one impression is foolish, and the healthy man because he has another is wise; but the one state requires to be changed into the other, the worse into the better.</div><div class="sentence"> As in education, a change of state has to be effected, and the sophist accomplishes by words the change which the physician works by the aid of drugs.</div><div class="sentence"> Not that any one ever made another think truly, who previously thought falsely.</div><div class="sentence"> For no one can think what is not, or, think anything different from that which he feels; and this is always true.</div><div class="sentence"> But as the inferior habit of mind has thoughts of kindred nature, so I conceive that a good mind causes men to have good thoughts; and these which the inexperienced call true, I maintain to be only better, and not truer than others.</div><div class="sentence"> And, O my dear Socrates, I do not call wise men tadpoles:  far from it; I say that they are the physicians of the human body, and the husbandmen of plants—for the husbandmen also take away the evil and disordered sensations of plants, and infuse into them good and healthy sensations—aye and true ones; and the wise and good rhetoricians make the good instead of the evil to seem just to states; for whatever appears to a state to be just and fair, so long as it is regarded as such, is just and fair to it; but the teacher of wisdom causes the good to take the place of the evil, both in appearance and in reality.</div><div class="sentence"> And in like manner the Sophist who is able to train his pupils in this spirit is a wise man, and deserves to be well paid by them.</div><div class="sentence"> And so one man is wiser than another; and no one thinks falsely, and you, whether you will or not, must endure to be a measure.</div><div class="sentence"> On these foundations the argument stands firm, which you, Socrates, may, if you please, overthrow by an opposite argument, or if you like you may put questions to me—a method to which no intelligent person will object, quite the reverse.</div><div class="sentence"> But I must beg you to put fair questions:  for there is great inconsistency in saying that you have a zeal for virtue, and then always behaving unfairly in argument.</div><div class="sentence"> The unfairness of which I complain is that you do not distinguish between mere disputation and dialectic:  the disputer may trip up his opponent as often as he likes, and make fun; but the dialectician will be in earnest, and only correct his adversary when necessary, telling him the errors into which he has fallen through his own fault, or that of the company which he has previously kept.</div><div class="sentence"> If you do so, your adversary will lay the blame of his own confusion and perplexity on himself, and not on you.</div><div class="sentence"> He will follow and love you, and will hate himself, and escape from himself into philosophy, in order that he may become different from what he was.</div><div class="sentence"> But the other mode of arguing, which is practised by the many, will have just the opposite effect upon him; and as he grows older, instead of turning philosopher, he will come to hate philosophy.</div><div class="sentence"> I would recommend you, therefore, as I said before, not to encourage yourself in this polemical and controversial temper, but to find out, in a friendly and congenial spirit, what we really mean when we say that all things are in motion, and that to every individual and state what appears, is.</div><div class="sentence"> In this manner you will consider whether knowledge and sensation are the same or different, but you will not argue, as you were just now doing, from the customary use of names and words, which the vulgar pervert in all sorts of ways, causing infinite perplexity to one another.</div><div class="sentence"> Such, Theodorus, is the very slight help which I am able to offer to your old friend; had he been living, he would have helped himself in a far more gloriose style.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">358</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  You are jesting, Socrates; indeed, your defence of him has been most valorous.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">359</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Thank you, friend; and I hope that you observed Protagoras bidding us be serious, as the text, 'Man is the measure of all things,' was a solemn one; and he reproached us with making a boy the medium of discourse, and said that the boy's timidity was made to tell against his argument; he also declared that we made a joke of him.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">360</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  How could I fail to observe all that, Socrates?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">361</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, and shall we do as he says?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">362</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  By all means.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">363</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But if his wishes are to be regarded, you and I must take up the argument, and in all seriousness, and ask and answer one another, for you see that the rest of us are nothing but boys.</div><div class="sentence"> In no other way can we escape the imputation, that in our fresh analysis of his thesis we are making fun with boys.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">364</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Well, but is not Theaetetus better able to follow a philosophical enquiry than a great many men who have long beards?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">365</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, Theodorus, but not better than you; and therefore please not to imagine that I am to defend by every means in my power your departed friend; and that you are to defend nothing and nobody.</div><div class="sentence"> At any rate, my good man, do not sheer off until we know whether you are a true measure of diagrams, or whether all men are equally measures and sufficient for themselves in astronomy and geometry, and the other branches of knowledge in which you are supposed to excel them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">366</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  He who is sitting by you, Socrates, will not easily avoid being drawn into an argument; and when I said just now that you would excuse me, and not, like the Lacedaemonians, compel me to strip and fight, I was talking nonsense—I should rather compare you to Scirrhon, who threw travellers from the rocks; for the Lacedaemonian rule is 'strip or depart,' but you seem to go about your work more after the fashion of Antaeus:  you will not allow any one who approaches you to depart until you have stripped him, and he has been compelled to try a fall with you in argument.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">367</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  There, Theodorus, you have hit off precisely the nature of my complaint; but I am even more pugnacious than the giants of old, for I have met with no end of heroes; many a Heracles, many a Theseus, mighty in words, has broken my head; nevertheless I am always at this rough exercise, which inspires me like a passion.</div><div class="sentence"> Please, then, to try a fall with me, whereby you will do yourself good as well as me.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">368</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  I consent; lead me whither you will, for I know that you are like destiny; no man can escape from any argument which you may weave for him.</div><div class="sentence"> But I am not disposed to go further than you suggest.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">369</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Once will be enough; and now take particular care that we do not again unwittingly expose ourselves to the reproach of talking childishly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">370</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  I will do my best to avoid that error.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">371</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In the first place, let us return to our old objection, and see whether we were right in blaming and taking offence at Protagoras on the ground that he assumed all to be equal and sufficient in wisdom; although he admitted that there was a better and worse, and that in respect of this, some who as he said were the wise excelled others.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">372</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">373</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Had Protagoras been living and answered for himself, instead of our answering for him, there would have been no need of our reviewing or reinforcing the argument.</div><div class="sentence"> But as he is not here, and some one may accuse us of speaking without authority on his behalf, had we not better come to a clearer agreement about his meaning, for a great deal may be at stake?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">374</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">375</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then let us obtain, not through any third person, but from his own statement and in the fewest words possible, the basis of agreement.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">376</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  In what way?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">377</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In this way: —His words are, 'What seems to a man, is to him.'</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">378</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, so he says.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">379</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And are not we, Protagoras, uttering the opinion of man, or rather of all mankind, when we say that every one thinks himself wiser than other men in some things, and their inferior in others?</div><div class="sentence"> In the hour of danger, when they are in perils of war, or of the sea, or of sickness, do they not look up to their commanders as if they were gods, and expect salvation from them, only because they excel them in knowledge?</div><div class="sentence"> Is not the world full of men in their several employments, who are looking for teachers and rulers of themselves and of the animals?</div><div class="sentence"> and there are plenty who think that they are able to teach and able to rule.</div><div class="sentence"> Now, in all this is implied that ignorance and wisdom exist among them, at least in their own opinion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">380</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">381</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And wisdom is assumed by them to be true thought, and ignorance to be false opinion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">382</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Exactly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">383</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  How then, Protagoras, would you have us treat the argument?</div><div class="sentence"> Shall we say that the opinions of men are always true, or sometimes true and sometimes false?</div><div class="sentence"> In either case, the result is the same, and their opinions are not always true, but sometimes true and sometimes false.</div><div class="sentence"> For tell me, Theodorus, do you suppose that you yourself, or any other follower of Protagoras, would contend that no one deems another ignorant or mistaken in his opinion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">384</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  The thing is incredible, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">385</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And yet that absurdity is necessarily involved in the thesis which declares man to be the measure of all things.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">386</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  How so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">387</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Why, suppose that you determine in your own mind something to be true, and declare your opinion to me; let us assume, as he argues, that this is true to you.</div><div class="sentence"> Now, if so, you must either say that the rest of us are not the judges of this opinion or judgment of yours, or that we judge you always to have a true opinion?</div><div class="sentence"> But are there not thousands upon thousands who, whenever you form a judgment, take up arms against you and are of an opposite judgment and opinion, deeming that you judge falsely?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">388</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, indeed, Socrates, thousands and tens of thousands, as Homer says, who give me a world of trouble.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">389</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, but are we to assert that what you think is true to you and false to the ten thousand others?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">390</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  No other inference seems to be possible.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">391</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And how about Protagoras himself?</div><div class="sentence"> If neither he nor the multitude thought, as indeed they do not think, that man is the measure of all things, must it not follow that the truth of which Protagoras wrote would be true to no one?</div><div class="sentence"> But if you suppose that he himself thought this, and that the multitude does not agree with him, you must begin by allowing that in whatever proportion the many are more than one, in that proportion his truth is more untrue than true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">392</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  That would follow if the truth is supposed to vary with individual opinion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">393</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the best of the joke is, that he acknowledges the truth of their opinion who believe his own opinion to be false; for he admits that the opinions of all men are true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">394</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">395</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And does he not allow that his own opinion is false, if he admits that the opinion of those who think him false is true?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">396</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of course.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">397</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Whereas the other side do not admit that they speak falsely?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">398</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  They do not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">399</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And he, as may be inferred from his writings, agrees that this opinion is also true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">400</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">401</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then all mankind, beginning with Protagoras, will contend, or rather, I should say that he will allow, when he concedes that his adversary has a true opinion—Protagoras, I say, will himself allow that neither a dog nor any ordinary man is the measure of anything which he has not learned—am I not right?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">402</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">403</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the truth of Protagoras being doubted by all, will be true neither to himself to any one else?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">404</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  I think, Socrates, that we are running my old friend too hard.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">405</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But I do not know that we are going beyond the truth.</div><div class="sentence"> Doubtless, as he is older, he may be expected to be wiser than we are.</div><div class="sentence"> And if he could only just get his head out of the world below, he would have overthrown both of us again and again, me for talking nonsense and you for assenting to me, and have been off and underground in a trice.</div><div class="sentence"> But as he is not within call, we must make the best use of our own faculties, such as they are, and speak out what appears to us to be true.</div><div class="sentence"> And one thing which no one will deny is, that there are great differences in the understandings of men.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">406</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  In that opinion I quite agree.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">407</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is there not most likely to be firm ground in the distinction which we were indicating on behalf of Protagoras, viz.</div><div class="sentence"> that most things, and all immediate sensations, such as hot, dry, sweet, are only such as they appear; if however difference of opinion is to be allowed at all, surely we must allow it in respect of health or disease?</div><div class="sentence"> for every woman, child, or living creature has not such a knowledge of what conduces to health as to enable them to cure themselves.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">408</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  I quite agree.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">409</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Or again, in politics, while affirming that just and unjust, honourable and disgraceful, holy and unholy, are in reality to each state such as the state thinks and makes lawful, and that in determining these matters no individual or state is wiser than another, still the followers of Protagoras will not deny that in determining what is or is not expedient for the community one state is wiser and one counsellor better than another—they will scarcely venture to maintain, that what a city enacts in the belief that it is expedient will always be really expedient.</div><div class="sentence"> But in the other case, I mean when they speak of justice and injustice, piety and impiety, they are confident that in nature these have no existence or essence of their own—the truth is that which is agreed on at the time of the agreement, and as long as the agreement lasts; and this is the philosophy of many who do not altogether go along with Protagoras.</div><div class="sentence"> Here arises a new question, Theodorus, which threatens to be more serious than the last.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">410</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Well, Socrates, we have plenty of leisure.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">411</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That is true, and your remark recalls to my mind an observation which I have often made, that those who have passed their days in the pursuit of philosophy are ridiculously at fault when they have to appear and speak in court.</div><div class="sentence"> How natural is this!</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">412</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">413</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I mean to say, that those who have been trained in philosophy and liberal pursuits are as unlike those who from their youth upwards have been knocking about in the courts and such places, as a freeman is in breeding unlike a slave.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">414</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  In what is the difference seen?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">415</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In the leisure spoken of by you, which a freeman can always command:  he has his talk out in peace, and, like ourselves, he wanders at will from one subject to another, and from a second to a third,—if the fancy takes him, he begins again, as we are doing now, caring not whether his words are many or few; his only aim is to attain the truth.</div><div class="sentence"> But the lawyer is always in a hurry; there is the water of the clepsydra driving him on, and not allowing him to expatiate at will:  and there is his adversary standing over him, enforcing his rights; the indictment, which in their phraseology is termed the affidavit, is recited at the time:  and from this he must not deviate.</div><div class="sentence"> He is a servant, and is continually disputing about a fellow-servant before his master, who is seated, and has the cause in his hands; the trial is never about some indifferent matter, but always concerns himself; and often the race is for his life.</div><div class="sentence"> The consequence has been, that he has become keen and shrewd; he has learned how to flatter his master in word and indulge him in deed; but his soul is small and unrighteous.</div><div class="sentence"> His condition, which has been that of a slave from his youth upwards, has deprived him of growth and uprightness and independence; dangers and fears, which were too much for his truth and honesty, came upon him in early years, when the tenderness of youth was unequal to them, and he has been driven into crooked ways; from the first he has practised deception and retaliation, and has become stunted and warped.</div><div class="sentence"> And so he has passed out of youth into manhood, having no soundness in him; and is now, as he thinks, a master in wisdom.</div><div class="sentence"> Such is the lawyer, Theodorus.</div><div class="sentence"> Will you have the companion picture of the philosopher, who is of our brotherhood; or shall we return to the argument?</div><div class="sentence"> Do not let us abuse the freedom of digression which we claim.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">416</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Nay, Socrates, not until we have finished what we are about; for you truly said that we belong to a brotherhood which is free, and are not the servants of the argument; but the argument is our servant, and must wait our leisure.</div><div class="sentence"> Who is our judge?</div><div class="sentence"> Or where is the spectator having any right to censure or control us, as he might the poets?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">417</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then, as this is your wish, I will describe the leaders; for there is no use in talking about the inferior sort.</div><div class="sentence"> In the first place, the lords of philosophy have never, from their youth upwards, known their way to the Agora, or the dicastery, or the council, or any other political assembly; they neither see nor hear the laws or decrees, as they are called, of the state written or recited; the eagerness of political societies in the attainment of offices—clubs, and banquets, and revels, and singing-maidens,—do not enter even into their dreams.</div><div class="sentence"> Whether any event has turned out well or ill in the city, what disgrace may have descended to any one from his ancestors, male or female, are matters of which the philosopher no more knows than he can tell, as they say, how many pints are contained in the ocean.</div><div class="sentence"> Neither is he conscious of his ignorance.</div><div class="sentence"> For he does not hold aloof in order that he may gain a reputation; but the truth is, that the outer form of him only is in the city:  his mind, disdaining the littlenesses and nothingnesses of human things, is 'flying all abroad' as Pindar says, measuring earth and heaven and the things which are under and on the earth and above the heaven, interrogating the whole nature of each and all in their entirety, but not condescending to anything which is within reach.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">418</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean, Socrates?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">419</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I will illustrate my meaning, Theodorus, by the jest which the clever witty Thracian handmaid is said to have made about Thales, when he fell into a well as he was looking up at the stars.</div><div class="sentence"> She said, that he was so eager to know what was going on in heaven, that he could not see what was before his feet.</div><div class="sentence"> This is a jest which is equally applicable to all philosophers.</div><div class="sentence"> For the philosopher is wholly unacquainted with his next-door neighbour; he is ignorant, not only of what he is doing, but he hardly knows whether he is a man or an animal; he is searching into the essence of man, and busy in enquiring what belongs to such a nature to do or suffer different from any other;—I think that you understand me, Theodorus?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">420</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  I do, and what you say is true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">421</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And thus, my friend, on every occasion, private as well as public, as I said at first, when he appears in a law-court, or in any place in which he has to speak of things which are at his feet and before his eyes, he is the jest, not only of Thracian handmaids but of the general herd, tumbling into wells and every sort of disaster through his inexperience.</div><div class="sentence"> His awkwardness is fearful, and gives the impression of imbecility.</div><div class="sentence"> When he is reviled, he has nothing personal to say in answer to the civilities of his adversaries, for he knows no scandals of any one, and they do not interest him; and therefore he is laughed at for his sheepishness; and when others are being praised and glorified, in the simplicity of his heart he cannot help going into fits of laughter, so that he seems to be a downright idiot.</div><div class="sentence"> When he hears a tyrant or king eulogized, he fancies that he is listening to the praises of some keeper of cattle—a swineherd, or shepherd, or perhaps a cowherd, who is congratulated on the quantity of milk which he squeezes from them; and he remarks that the creature whom they tend, and out of whom they squeeze the wealth, is of a less tractable and more insidious nature.</div><div class="sentence"> Then, again, he observes that the great man is of necessity as ill-mannered and uneducated as any shepherd—for he has no leisure, and he is surrounded by a wall, which is his mountain-pen.</div><div class="sentence"> Hearing of enormous landed proprietors of ten thousand acres and more, our philosopher deems this to be a trifle, because he has been accustomed to think of the whole earth; and when they sing the praises of family, and say that some one is a gentleman because he can show seven generations of wealthy ancestors, he thinks that their sentiments only betray a dull and narrow vision in those who utter them, and who are not educated enough to look at the whole, nor to consider that every man has had thousands and ten thousands of progenitors, and among them have been rich and poor, kings and slaves, Hellenes and barbarians, innumerable.</div><div class="sentence"> And when people pride themselves on having a pedigree of twenty-five ancestors, which goes back to Heracles, the son of Amphitryon, he cannot understand their poverty of ideas.</div><div class="sentence"> Why are they unable to calculate that Amphitryon had a twenty-fifth ancestor, who might have been anybody, and was such as fortune made him, and he had a fiftieth, and so on?</div><div class="sentence"> He amuses himself with the notion that they cannot count, and thinks that a little arithmetic would have got rid of their senseless vanity.</div><div class="sentence"> Now, in all these cases our philosopher is derided by the vulgar, partly because he is thought to despise them, and also because he is ignorant of what is before him, and always at a loss.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">422</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  That is very true, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">423</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But, O my friend, when he draws the other into upper air, and gets him out of his pleas and rejoinders into the contemplation of justice and injustice in their own nature and in their difference from one another and from all other things; or from the commonplaces about the happiness of a king or of a rich man to the consideration of government, and of human happiness and misery in general—what they are, and how a man is to attain the one and avoid the other—when that narrow, keen, little legal mind is called to account about all this, he gives the philosopher his revenge; for dizzied by the height at which he is hanging, whence he looks down into space, which is a strange experience to him, he being dismayed, and lost, and stammering broken words, is laughed at, not by Thracian handmaidens or any other uneducated persons, for they have no eye for the situation, but by every man who has not been brought up a slave.</div><div class="sentence"> Such are the two characters, Theodorus:  the one of the freeman, who has been trained in liberty and leisure, whom you call the philosopher,—him we cannot blame because he appears simple and of no account when he has to perform some menial task, such as packing up bed-clothes, or flavouring a sauce or fawning speech; the other character is that of the man who is able to do all this kind of service smartly and neatly, but knows not how to wear his cloak like a gentleman; still less with the music of discourse can he hymn the true life aright which is lived by immortals or men blessed of heaven.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">424</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  If you could only persuade everybody, Socrates, as you do me, of the truth of your words, there would be more peace and fewer evils among men.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">425</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Evils, Theodorus, can never pass away; for there must always remain something which is antagonistic to good.</div><div class="sentence"> Having no place among the gods in heaven, of necessity they hover around the mortal nature, and this earthly sphere.</div><div class="sentence"> Wherefore we ought to fly away from earth to heaven as quickly as we can; and to fly away is to become like God, as far as this is possible; and to become like him, is to become holy, just, and wise.</div><div class="sentence"> But, O my friend, you cannot easily convince mankind that they should pursue virtue or avoid vice, not merely in order that a man may seem to be good, which is the reason given by the world, and in my judgment is only a repetition of an old wives' fable.</div><div class="sentence"> Whereas, the truth is that God is never in any way unrighteous—he is perfect righteousness; and he of us who is the most righteous is most like him.</div><div class="sentence"> Herein is seen the true cleverness of a man, and also his nothingness and want of manhood.</div><div class="sentence"> For to know this is true wisdom and virtue, and ignorance of this is manifest folly and vice.</div><div class="sentence"> All other kinds of wisdom or cleverness, which seem only, such as the wisdom of politicians, or the wisdom of the arts, are coarse and vulgar.</div><div class="sentence"> The unrighteous man, or the sayer and doer of unholy things, had far better not be encouraged in the illusion that his roguery is clever; for men glory in their shame—they fancy that they hear others saying of them, 'These are not mere good-for-nothing persons, mere burdens of the earth, but such as men should be who mean to dwell safely in a state.'</div><div class="sentence"> Let us tell them that they are all the more truly what they do not think they are because they do not know it; for they do not know the penalty of injustice, which above all things they ought to know—not stripes and death, as they suppose, which evil-doers often escape, but a penalty which cannot be escaped.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">426</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  What is that?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">427</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  There are two patterns eternally set before them; the one blessed and divine, the other godless and wretched:  but they do not see them, or perceive that in their utter folly and infatuation they are growing like the one and unlike the other, by reason of their evil deeds; and the penalty is, that they lead a life answering to the pattern which they are growing like.</div><div class="sentence"> And if we tell them, that unless they depart from their cunning, the place of innocence will not receive them after death; and that here on earth, they will live ever in the likeness of their own evil selves, and with evil friends—when they hear this they in their superior cunning will seem to be listening to the talk of idiots.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">428</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">429</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Too true, my friend, as I well know; there is, however, one peculiarity in their case:  when they begin to reason in private about their dislike of philosophy, if they have the courage to hear the argument out, and do not run away, they grow at last strangely discontented with themselves; their rhetoric fades away, and they become helpless as children.</div><div class="sentence"> These however are digressions from which we must now desist, or they will overflow, and drown the original argument; to which, if you please, we will now return.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">430</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  For my part, Socrates, I would rather have the digressions, for at my age I find them easier to follow; but if you wish, let us go back to the argument.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">431</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Had we not reached the point at which the partisans of the perpetual flux, who say that things are as they seem to each one, were confidently maintaining that the ordinances which the state commanded and thought just, were just to the state which imposed them, while they were in force; this was especially asserted of justice; but as to the good, no one had any longer the hardihood to contend of any ordinances which the state thought and enacted to be good that these, while they were in force, were really good;—he who said so would be playing with the name 'good,' and would not touch the real question—it would be a mockery, would it not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">432</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly it would.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">433</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  He ought not to speak of the name, but of the thing which is contemplated under the name.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">434</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">435</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Whatever be the term used, the good or expedient is the aim of legislation, and as far as she has an opinion, the state imposes all laws with a view to the greatest expediency; can legislation have any other aim?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">436</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">437</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But is the aim attained always?</div><div class="sentence"> do not mistakes often happen?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">438</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, I think that there are mistakes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">439</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The possibility of error will be more distinctly recognised, if we put the question in reference to the whole class under which the good or expedient falls.</div><div class="sentence"> That whole class has to do with the future, and laws are passed under the idea that they will be useful in after-time; which, in other words, is the future.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">440</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">441</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Suppose now, that we ask Protagoras, or one of his disciples, a question: —O, Protagoras, we will say to him, Man is, as you declare, the measure of all things—white, heavy, light:  of all such things he is the judge; for he has the criterion of them in himself, and when he thinks that things are such as he experiences them to be, he thinks what is and is true to himself.</div><div class="sentence"> Is it not so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">442</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">443</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And do you extend your doctrine, Protagoras (as we shall further say), to the future as well as to the present; and has he the criterion not only of what in his opinion is but of what will be, and do things always happen to him as he expected?</div><div class="sentence"> For example, take the case of heat: —When an ordinary man thinks that he is going to have a fever, and that this kind of heat is coming on, and another person, who is a physician, thinks the contrary, whose opinion is likely to prove right?</div><div class="sentence"> Or are they both right?—he will have a heat and fever in his own judgment, and not have a fever in the physician's judgment?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">444</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  How ludicrous!</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">445</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the vinegrower, if I am not mistaken, is a better judge of the sweetness or dryness of the vintage which is not yet gathered than the harp-player?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">446</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">447</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And in musical composition the musician will know better than the training master what the training master himself will hereafter think harmonious or the reverse?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">448</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of course.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">449</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the cook will be a better judge than the guest, who is not a cook, of the pleasure to be derived from the dinner which is in preparation; for of present or past pleasure we are not as yet arguing; but can we say that every one will be to himself the best judge of the pleasure which will seem to be and will be to him in the future?—nay, would not you, Protagoras, better guess which arguments in a court would convince any one of us than the ordinary man?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">450</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly, Socrates, he used to profess in the strongest manner that he was the superior of all men in this respect.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">451</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  To be sure, friend:  who would have paid a large sum for the privilege of talking to him, if he had really persuaded his visitors that neither a prophet nor any other man was better able to judge what will be and seem to be in the future than every one could for himself?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">452</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Who indeed?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">453</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And legislation and expediency are all concerned with the future; and every one will admit that states, in passing laws, must often fail of their highest interests?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">454</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Quite true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">455</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then we may fairly argue against your master, that he must admit one man to be wiser than another, and that the wiser is a measure:  but I, who know nothing, am not at all obliged to accept the honour which the advocate of Protagoras was just now forcing upon me, whether I would or not, of being a measure of anything.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">456</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  That is the best refutation of him, Socrates; although he is also caught when he ascribes truth to the opinions of others, who give the lie direct to his own opinion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">457</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  There are many ways, Theodorus, in which the doctrine that every opinion of every man is true may be refuted; but there is more difficulty in proving that states of feeling, which are present to a man, and out of which arise sensations and opinions in accordance with them, are also untrue.</div><div class="sentence"> And very likely I have been talking nonsense about them; for they may be unassailable, and those who say that there is clear evidence of them, and that they are matters of knowledge, may probably be right; in which case our friend Theaetetus was not so far from the mark when he identified perception and knowledge.</div><div class="sentence"> And therefore let us draw nearer, as the advocate of Protagoras desires; and give the truth of the universal flux a ring:  is the theory sound or not?</div><div class="sentence"> at any rate, no small war is raging about it, and there are combination not a few.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">458</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  No small, war, indeed, for in Ionia the sect makes rapid strides; the disciples of Heracleitus are most energetic upholders of the doctrine.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">459</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then we are the more bound, my dear Theodorus, to examine the question from the foundation as it is set forth by themselves.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">460</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly we are.</div><div class="sentence"> About these speculations of Heracleitus, which, as you say, are as old as Homer, or even older still, the Ephesians themselves, who profess to know them, are downright mad, and you cannot talk with them on the subject.</div><div class="sentence"> For, in accordance with their text-books, they are always in motion; but as for dwelling upon an argument or a question, and quietly asking and answering in turn, they can no more do so than they can fly; or rather, the determination of these fellows not to have a particle of rest in them is more than the utmost powers of negation can express.</div><div class="sentence"> If you ask any of them a question, he will produce, as from a quiver, sayings brief and dark, and shoot them at you; and if you inquire the reason of what he has said, you will be hit by some other new-fangled word, and will make no way with any of them, nor they with one another; their great care is, not to allow of any settled principle either in their arguments or in their minds, conceiving, as I imagine, that any such principle would be stationary; for they are at war with the stationary, and do what they can to drive it out everywhere.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">461</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I suppose, Theodorus, that you have only seen them when they were fighting, and have never stayed with them in time of peace, for they are no friends of yours; and their peace doctrines are only communicated by them at leisure, as I imagine, to those disciples of theirs whom they want to make like themselves.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">462</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Disciples!</div><div class="sentence"> my good sir, they have none; men of their sort are not one another's disciples, but they grow up at their own sweet will, and get their inspiration anywhere, each of them saying of his neighbour that he knows nothing.</div><div class="sentence"> From these men, then, as I was going to remark, you will never get a reason, whether with their will or without their will; we must take the question out of their hands, and make the analysis ourselves, as if we were doing geometrical problem.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">463</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Quite right too; but as touching the aforesaid problem, have we not heard from the ancients, who concealed their wisdom from the many in poetical figures, that Oceanus and Tethys, the origin of all things, are streams, and that nothing is at rest?</div><div class="sentence"> And now the moderns, in their superior wisdom, have declared the same openly, that the cobbler too may hear and learn of them, and no longer foolishly imagine that some things are at rest and others in motion—having learned that all is motion, he will duly honour his teachers.</div><div class="sentence"> I had almost forgotten the opposite doctrine, Theodorus,</div></div>
