<div class="speech"><span class="ref">464</span> <div class="sentence">  This is the language of Parmenides, Melissus, and their followers, who stoutly maintain that all being is one and self-contained, and has no place in which to move.</div><div class="sentence"> What shall we do, friend, with all these people; for, advancing step by step, we have imperceptibly got between the combatants, and, unless we can protect our retreat, we shall pay the penalty of our rashness—like the players in the palaestra who are caught upon the line, and are dragged different ways by the two parties.</div><div class="sentence"> Therefore I think that we had better begin by considering those whom we first accosted, 'the river-gods,' and, if we find any truth in them, we will help them to pull us over, and try to get away from the others.</div><div class="sentence"> But if the partisans of 'the whole' appear to speak more truly, we will fly off from the party which would move the immovable, to them.</div><div class="sentence"> And if I find that neither of them have anything reasonable to say, we shall be in a ridiculous position, having so great a conceit of our own poor opinion and rejecting that of ancient and famous men.</div><div class="sentence"> O Theodorus, do you think that there is any use in proceeding when the danger is so great?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">465</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Nay, Socrates, not to examine thoroughly what the two parties have to say would be quite intolerable.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">466</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then examine we must, since you, who were so reluctant to begin, are so eager to proceed.</div><div class="sentence"> The nature of motion appears to be the question with which we begin.</div><div class="sentence"> What do they mean when they say that all things are in motion?</div><div class="sentence"> Is there only one kind of motion, or, as I rather incline to think, two?</div><div class="sentence"> I should like to have your opinion upon this point in addition to my own, that I may err, if I must err, in your company; tell me, then, when a thing changes from one place to another, or goes round in the same place, is not that what is called motion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">467</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">468</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Here then we have one kind of motion.</div><div class="sentence"> But when a thing, remaining on the same spot, grows old, or becomes black from being white, or hard from being soft, or undergoes any other change, may not this be properly called motion of another kind?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">469</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  I think so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">470</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Say rather that it must be so.</div><div class="sentence"> Of motion then there are these two kinds, 'change,' and 'motion in place.'</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">471</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  You are right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">472</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And now, having made this distinction, let us address ourselves to those who say that all is motion, and ask them whether all things according to them have the two kinds of motion, and are changed as well as move in place, or is one thing moved in both ways, and another in one only?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">473</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Indeed, I do not know what to answer; but I think they would say that all things are moved in both ways.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">474</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, comrade; for, if not, they would have to say that the same things are in motion and at rest, and there would be no more truth in saying that all things are in motion, than that all things are at rest.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">475</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  To be sure.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">476</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And if they are to be in motion, and nothing is to be devoid of motion, all things must always have every sort of motion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">477</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Most true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">478</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Consider a further point:  did we not understand them to explain the generation of heat, whiteness, or anything else, in some such manner as the following: —were they not saying that each of them is moving between the agent and the patient, together with a perception, and that the patient ceases to be a perceiving power and becomes a percipient, and the agent a quale instead of a quality?</div><div class="sentence"> I suspect that quality may appear a strange and uncouth term to you, and that you do not understand the abstract expression.</div><div class="sentence"> Then I will take concrete instances:  I mean to say that the producing power or agent becomes neither heat nor whiteness but hot and white, and the like of other things.</div><div class="sentence"> For I must repeat what I said before, that neither the agent nor patient have any absolute existence, but when they come together and generate sensations and their objects, the one becomes a thing of a certain quality, and the other a percipient.</div><div class="sentence"> You remember?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">479</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of course.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">480</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  We may leave the details of their theory unexamined, but we must not forget to ask them the only question with which we are concerned:  Are all things in motion and flux?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">481</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, they will reply.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">482</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And they are moved in both those ways which we distinguished, that is to say, they move in place and are also changed?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">483</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of course, if the motion is to be perfect.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">484</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  If they only moved in place and were not changed, we should be able to say what is the nature of the things which are in motion and flux?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">485</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Exactly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">486</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But now, since not even white continues to flow white, and whiteness itself is a flux or change which is passing into another colour, and is never to be caught standing still, can the name of any colour be rightly used at all?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">487</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  How is that possible, Socrates, either in the case of this or of any other quality—if while we are using the word the object is escaping in the flux?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">488</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And what would you say of perceptions, such as sight and hearing, or any other kind of perception?</div><div class="sentence"> Is there any stopping in the act of seeing and hearing?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">489</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not, if all things are in motion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">490</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then we must not speak of seeing any more than of not-seeing, nor of any other perception more than of any non-perception, if all things partake of every kind of motion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">491</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">492</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yet perception is knowledge:  so at least Theaetetus and I were saying.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">493</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">494</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then when we were asked what is knowledge, we no more answered what is knowledge than what is not knowledge?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">495</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  I suppose not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">496</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Here, then, is a fine result:  we corrected our first answer in our eagerness to prove that nothing is at rest.</div><div class="sentence"> But if nothing is at rest, every answer upon whatever subject is equally right:  you may say that a thing is or is not thus; or, if you prefer, 'becomes' thus; and if we say 'becomes,' we shall not then hamper them with words expressive of rest.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">497</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Quite true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">498</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, Theodorus, except in saying 'thus' and 'not thus.'</div><div class="sentence"> But you ought not to use the word 'thus,' for there is no motion in 'thus' or in 'not thus.'</div><div class="sentence"> The maintainers of the doctrine have as yet no words in which to express themselves, and must get a new language.</div><div class="sentence"> I know of no word that will suit them, except perhaps 'no how,' which is perfectly indefinite.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">499</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, that is a manner of speaking in which they will be quite at home.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">500</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And so, Theodorus, we have got rid of your friend without assenting to his doctrine, that every man is the measure of all things—a wise man only is a measure; neither can we allow that knowledge is perception, certainly not on the hypothesis of a perpetual flux, unless perchance our friend Theaetetus is able to convince us that it is.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">501</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very good, Socrates; and now that the argument about the doctrine of Protagoras has been completed, I am absolved from answering; for this was the agreement.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">502</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Not, Theodorus, until you and Socrates have discussed the doctrine of those who say that all things are at rest, as you were proposing.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">503</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  You, Theaetetus, who are a young rogue, must not instigate your elders to a breach of faith, but should prepare to answer Socrates in the remainder of the argument.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">504</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, if he wishes; but I would rather have heard about the doctrine of rest.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">505</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Invite Socrates to an argument—invite horsemen to the open plain; do but ask him, and he will answer.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">506</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Nevertheless, Theodorus, I am afraid that I shall not be able to comply with the request of Theaetetus.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">507</span><div class="speaker">THEODORUS</div><div class="sentence">  Not comply!</div><div class="sentence"> for what reason?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">508</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  My reason is that I have a kind of reverence; not so much for Melissus and the others, who say that 'All is one and at rest,' as for the great leader himself, Parmenides, venerable and awful, as in Homeric language he may be called;—him I should be ashamed to approach in a spirit unworthy of him.</div><div class="sentence"> I met him when he was an old man, and I was a mere youth, and he appeared to me to have a glorious depth of mind.</div><div class="sentence"> And I am afraid that we may not understand his words, and may be still further from understanding his meaning; above all I fear that the nature of knowledge, which is the main subject of our discussion, may be thrust out of sight by the unbidden guests who will come pouring in upon our feast of discourse, if we let them in—besides, the question which is now stirring is of immense extent, and will be treated unfairly if only considered by the way; or if treated adequately and at length, will put into the shade the other question of knowledge.</div><div class="sentence"> Neither the one nor the other can be allowed; but I must try by my art of midwifery to deliver Theaetetus of his conceptions about knowledge.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">509</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very well; do so if you will.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">510</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then now, Theaetetus, take another view of the subject:  you answered that knowledge is perception?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">511</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I did.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">512</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And if any one were to ask you:  With what does a man see black and white colours?</div><div class="sentence"> and with what does he hear high and low sounds?—you would say, if I am not mistaken, 'With the eyes and with the ears.'</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">513</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">514</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The free use of words and phrases, rather than minute precision, is generally characteristic of a liberal education, and the opposite is pedantic; but sometimes precision is necessary, and I believe that the answer which you have just given is open to the charge of incorrectness; for which is more correct, to say that we see or hear with the eyes and with the ears, or through the eyes and through the ears.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">515</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should say 'through,' Socrates, rather than 'with.'</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">516</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, my boy, for no one can suppose that in each of us, as in a sort of Trojan horse, there are perched a number of unconnected senses, which do not all meet in some one nature, the mind, or whatever we please to call it, of which they are the instruments, and with which through them we perceive objects of sense.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">517</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I agree with you in that opinion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">518</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The reason why I am thus precise is, because I want to know whether, when we perceive black and white through the eyes, and again, other qualities through other organs, we do not perceive them with one and the same part of ourselves, and, if you were asked, you might refer all such perceptions to the body.</div><div class="sentence"> Perhaps, however, I had better allow you to answer for yourself and not interfere.</div><div class="sentence"> Tell me, then, are not the organs through which you perceive warm and hard and light and sweet, organs of the body?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">519</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of the body, certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">520</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And you would admit that what you perceive through one faculty you cannot perceive through another; the objects of hearing, for example, cannot be perceived through sight, or the objects of sight through hearing?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">521</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of course not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">522</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  If you have any thought about both of them, this common perception cannot come to you, either through the one or the other organ?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">523</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  It cannot.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">524</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  How about sounds and colours:  in the first place you would admit that they both exist?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">525</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">526</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And that either of them is different from the other, and the same with itself?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">527</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">528</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And that both are two and each of them one?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">529</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">530</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You can further observe whether they are like or unlike one another?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">531</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I dare say.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">532</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But through what do you perceive all this about them?</div><div class="sentence"> for neither through hearing nor yet through seeing can you apprehend that which they have in common.</div><div class="sentence"> Let me give you an illustration of the point at issue: —If there were any meaning in asking whether sounds and colours are saline or not, you would be able to tell me what faculty would consider the question.</div><div class="sentence"> It would not be sight or hearing, but some other.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">533</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly; the faculty of taste.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">534</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Very good; and now tell me what is the power which discerns, not only in sensible objects, but in all things, universal notions, such as those which are called being and not-being, and those others about which we were just asking—what organs will you assign for the perception of these notions?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">535</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  You are thinking of being and not being, likeness and unlikeness, sameness and difference, and also of unity and other numbers which are applied to objects of sense; and you mean to ask, through what bodily organ the soul perceives odd and even numbers and other arithmetical conceptions.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">536</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You follow me excellently, Theaetetus; that is precisely what I am asking.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">537</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Indeed, Socrates, I cannot answer; my only notion is, that these, unlike objects of sense, have no separate organ, but that the mind, by a power of her own, contemplates the universals in all things.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">538</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You are a beauty, Theaetetus, and not ugly, as Theodorus was saying; for he who utters the beautiful is himself beautiful and good.</div><div class="sentence"> And besides being beautiful, you have done me a kindness in releasing me from a very long discussion, if you are clear that the soul views some things by herself and others through the bodily organs.</div><div class="sentence"> For that was my own opinion, and I wanted you to agree with me.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">539</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I am quite clear.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">540</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And to which class would you refer being or essence; for this, of all our notions, is the most universal?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">541</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should say, to that class which the soul aspires to know of herself.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">542</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And would you say this also of like and unlike, same and other?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">543</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">544</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And would you say the same of the noble and base, and of good and evil?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">545</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  These I conceive to be notions which are essentially relative, and which the soul also perceives by comparing in herself things past and present with the future.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">546</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And does she not perceive the hardness of that which is hard by the touch, and the softness of that which is soft equally by the touch?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">547</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">548</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But their essence and what they are, and their opposition to one another, and the essential nature of this opposition, the soul herself endeavours to decide for us by the review and comparison of them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">549</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">550</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The simple sensations which reach the soul through the body are given at birth to men and animals by nature, but their reflections on the being and use of them are slowly and hardly gained, if they are ever gained, by education and long experience.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">551</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Assuredly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">552</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And can a man attain truth who fails of attaining being?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">553</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Impossible.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">554</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And can he who misses the truth of anything, have a knowledge of that thing?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">555</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  He cannot.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">556</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then knowledge does not consist in impressions of sense, but in reasoning about them; in that only, and not in the mere impression, truth and being can be attained?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">557</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">558</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And would you call the two processes by the same name, when there is so great a difference between them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">559</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  That would certainly not be right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">560</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And what name would you give to seeing, hearing, smelling, being cold and being hot?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">561</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should call all of them perceiving—what other name could be given to them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">562</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Perception would be the collective name of them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">563</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">564</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Which, as we say, has no part in the attainment of truth any more than of being?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">565</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">566</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And therefore not in science or knowledge?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">567</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  No.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">568</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then perception, Theaetetus, can never be the same as knowledge or science?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">569</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly not, Socrates; and knowledge has now been most distinctly proved to be different from perception.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">570</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But the original aim of our discussion was to find out rather what knowledge is than what it is not; at the same time we have made some progress, for we no longer seek for knowledge in perception at all, but in that other process, however called, in which the mind is alone and engaged with being.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">571</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  You mean, Socrates, if I am not mistaken, what is called thinking or opining.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">572</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You conceive truly.</div><div class="sentence"> And now, my friend, please to begin again at this point; and having wiped out of your memory all that has preceded, see if you have arrived at any clearer view, and once more say what is knowledge.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">573</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I cannot say, Socrates, that all opinion is knowledge, because there may be a false opinion; but I will venture to assert, that knowledge is true opinion:  let this then be my reply; and if this is hereafter disproved, I must try to find another.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">574</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That is the way in which you ought to answer, Theaetetus, and not in your former hesitating strain, for if we are bold we shall gain one of two advantages; either we shall find what we seek, or we shall be less likely to think that we know what we do not know—in either case we shall be richly rewarded.</div><div class="sentence"> And now, what are you saying?—Are there two sorts of opinion, one true and the other false; and do you define knowledge to be the true?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">575</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, according to my present view.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">576</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Is it still worth our while to resume the discussion touching opinion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">577</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  To what are you alluding?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">578</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  There is a point which often troubles me, and is a great perplexity to me, both in regard to myself and others.</div><div class="sentence"> I cannot make out the nature or origin of the mental experience to which I refer.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">579</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Pray what is it?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">580</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  How there can be false opinion—that difficulty still troubles the eye of my mind; and I am uncertain whether I shall leave the question, or begin over again in a new way.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">581</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Begin again, Socrates,—at least if you think that there is the slightest necessity for doing so.</div><div class="sentence"> Were not you and Theodorus just now remarking very truly, that in discussions of this kind we may take our own time?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">582</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You are quite right, and perhaps there will be no harm in retracing our steps and beginning again.</div><div class="sentence"> Better a little which is well done, than a great deal imperfectly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">583</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">584</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, and what is the difficulty?</div><div class="sentence"> Do we not speak of false opinion, and say that one man holds a false and another a true opinion, as though there were some natural distinction between them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">585</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  We certainly say so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">586</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  All things and everything are either known or not known.</div><div class="sentence"> I leave out of view the intermediate conceptions of learning and forgetting, because they have nothing to do with our present question.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">587</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  There can be no doubt, Socrates, if you exclude these, that there is no other alternative but knowing or not knowing a thing.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">588</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That point being now determined, must we not say that he who has an opinion, must have an opinion about something which he knows or does not know?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">589</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  He must.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">590</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  He who knows, cannot but know; and he who does not know, cannot know?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">591</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of course.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">592</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What shall we say then?</div><div class="sentence"> When a man has a false opinion does he think that which he knows to be some other thing which he knows, and knowing both, is he at the same time ignorant of both?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">593</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  That, Socrates, is impossible.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">594</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But perhaps he thinks of something which he does not know as some other thing which he does not know; for example, he knows neither Theaetetus nor Socrates, and yet he fancies that Theaetetus is Socrates, or Socrates Theaetetus?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">595</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  How can he?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">596</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But surely he cannot suppose what he knows to be what he does not know, or what he does not know to be what he knows?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">597</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  That would be monstrous.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">598</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Where, then, is false opinion?</div><div class="sentence"> For if all things are either known or unknown, there can be no opinion which is not comprehended under this alternative, and so false opinion is excluded.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">599</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Most true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">600</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Suppose that we remove the question out of the sphere of knowing or not knowing, into that of being and not-being.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">601</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">602</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  May we not suspect the simple truth to be that he who thinks about anything, that which is not, will necessarily think what is false, whatever in other respects may be the state of his mind?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">603</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  That, again, is not unlikely, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">604</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then suppose some one to say to us, Theaetetus: —Is it possible for any man to think that which is not, either as a self-existent substance or as a predicate of something else?</div><div class="sentence"> And suppose that we answer, 'Yes, he can, when he thinks what is not true.</div><div class="sentence"> '—That will be our answer?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">605</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">606</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But is there any parallel to this?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">607</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">608</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Can a man see something and yet see nothing?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">609</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Impossible.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">610</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But if he sees any one thing, he sees something that exists.</div><div class="sentence"> Do you suppose that what is one is ever to be found among non-existing things?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">611</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I do not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">612</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  He then who sees some one thing, sees something which is?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">613</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">614</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And he who hears anything, hears some one thing, and hears that which is?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">615</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">616</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And he who touches anything, touches something which is one and therefore is?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">617</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  That again is true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">618</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And does not he who thinks, think some one thing?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">619</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">620</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And does not he who thinks some one thing, think something which is?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">621</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I agree.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">622</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then he who thinks of that which is not, thinks of nothing?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">623</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">624</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And he who thinks of nothing, does not think at all?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">625</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Obviously.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">626</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then no one can think that which is not, either as a self-existent substance or as a predicate of something else?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">627</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">628</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then to think falsely is different from thinking that which is not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">629</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  It would seem so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">630</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then false opinion has no existence in us, either in the sphere of being or of knowledge?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">631</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">632</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But may not the following be the description of what we express by this name?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">633</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">634</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  May we not suppose that false opinion or thought is a sort of heterodoxy; a person may make an exchange in his mind, and say that one real object is another real object.</div><div class="sentence"> For thus he always thinks that which is, but he puts one thing in place of another; and missing the aim of his thoughts, he may be truly said to have false opinion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">635</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Now you appear to me to have spoken the exact truth:  when a man puts the base in the place of the noble, or the noble in the place of the base, then he has truly false opinion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">636</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I see, Theaetetus, that your fear has disappeared, and that you are beginning to despise me.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">637</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What makes you say so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">638</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You think, if I am not mistaken, that your 'truly false' is safe from censure, and that I shall never ask whether there can be a swift which is slow, or a heavy which is light, or any other self-contradictory thing, which works, not according to its own nature, but according to that of its opposite.</div><div class="sentence"> But I will not insist upon this, for I do not wish needlessly to discourage you.</div><div class="sentence"> And so you are satisfied that false opinion is heterodoxy, or the thought of something else?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">639</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I am.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">640</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  It is possible then upon your view for the mind to conceive of one thing as another?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">641</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">642</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But must not the mind, or thinking power, which misplaces them, have a conception either of both objects or of one of them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">643</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">644</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Either together or in succession?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">645</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very good.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">646</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And do you mean by conceiving, the same which I mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">647</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What is that?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">648</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I mean the conversation which the soul holds with herself in considering of anything.</div><div class="sentence"> I speak of what I scarcely understand; but the soul when thinking appears to me to be just talking—asking questions of herself and answering them, affirming and denying.</div><div class="sentence"> And when she has arrived at a decision, either gradually or by a sudden impulse, and has at last agreed, and does not doubt, this is called her opinion.</div><div class="sentence"> I say, then, that to form an opinion is to speak, and opinion is a word spoken,—I mean, to oneself and in silence, not aloud or to another:  What think you?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">649</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I agree.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">650</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then when any one thinks of one thing as another, he is saying to himself that one thing is another?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">651</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">652</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But do you ever remember saying to yourself that the noble is certainly base, or the unjust just; or, best of all—have you ever attempted to convince yourself that one thing is another?</div><div class="sentence"> Nay, not even in sleep, did you ever venture to say to yourself that odd is even, or anything of the kind?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">653</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Never.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">654</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And do you suppose that any other man, either in his senses or out of them, ever seriously tried to persuade himself that an ox is a horse, or that two are one?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">655</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">656</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But if thinking is talking to oneself, no one speaking and thinking of two objects, and apprehending them both in his soul, will say and think that the one is the other of them, and I must add, that even you, lover of dispute as you are, had better let the word 'other' alone (i.e.</div><div class="sentence"> not insist that 'one' and 'other' are the same (Both words in Greek are called eteron:  compare Parmen.</div><div class="sentence"> ; Euthyd.)).</div><div class="sentence"> I mean to say, that no one thinks the noble to be base, or anything of the kind.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">657</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I will give up the word 'other,' Socrates; and I agree to what you say.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">658</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  If a man has both of them in his thoughts, he cannot think that the one of them is the other?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">659</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">660</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Neither, if he has one of them only in his mind and not the other, can he think that one is the other?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">661</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True; for we should have to suppose that he apprehends that which is not in his thoughts at all.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">662</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then no one who has either both or only one of the two objects in his mind can think that the one is the other.</div><div class="sentence"> And therefore, he who maintains that false opinion is heterodoxy is talking nonsense; for neither in this, any more than in the previous way, can false opinion exist in us.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">663</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  No.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">664</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But if, Theaetetus, this is not admitted, we shall be driven into many absurdities.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">665</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What are they?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">666</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I will not tell you until I have endeavoured to consider the matter from every point of view.</div><div class="sentence"> For I should be ashamed of us if we were driven in our perplexity to admit the absurd consequences of which I speak.</div><div class="sentence"> But if we find the solution, and get away from them, we may regard them only as the difficulties of others, and the ridicule will not attach to us.</div><div class="sentence"> On the other hand, if we utterly fail, I suppose that we must be humble, and allow the argument to trample us under foot, as the sea-sick passenger is trampled upon by the sailor, and to do anything to us.</div><div class="sentence"> Listen, then, while I tell you how I hope to find a way out of our difficulty.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">667</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Let me hear.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">668</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I think that we were wrong in denying that a man could think what he knew to be what he did not know; and that there is a way in which such a deception is possible.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">669</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  You mean to say, as I suspected at the time, that I may know Socrates, and at a distance see some one who is unknown to me, and whom I mistake for him—then the deception will occur?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">670</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But has not that position been relinquished by us, because involving the absurdity that we should know and not know the things which we know?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">671</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">672</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let us make the assertion in another form, which may or may not have a favourable issue; but as we are in a great strait, every argument should be turned over and tested.</div><div class="sentence"> Tell me, then, whether I am right in saying that you may learn a thing which at one time you did not know?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">673</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly you may.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">674</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And another and another?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">675</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">676</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I would have you imagine, then, that there exists in the mind of man a block of wax, which is of different sizes in different men; harder, moister, and having more or less of purity in one than another, and in some of an intermediate quality.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">677</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I see.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">678</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let us say that this tablet is a gift of Memory, the mother of the Muses; and that when we wish to remember anything which we have seen, or heard, or thought in our own minds, we hold the wax to the perceptions and thoughts, and in that material receive the impression of them as from the seal of a ring; and that we remember and know what is imprinted as long as the image lasts; but when the image is effaced, or cannot be taken, then we forget and do not know.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">679</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very good.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">680</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Now, when a person has this knowledge, and is considering something which he sees or hears, may not false opinion arise in the following manner?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">681</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  In what manner?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">682</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  When he thinks what he knows, sometimes to be what he knows, and sometimes to be what he does not know.</div><div class="sentence"> We were wrong before in denying the possibility of this.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">683</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  And how would you amend the former statement?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">684</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I should begin by making a list of the impossible cases which must be excluded.</div><div class="sentence"> (1) No one can think one thing to be another when he does not perceive either of them, but has the memorial or seal of both of them in his mind; nor can any mistaking of one thing for another occur, when he only knows one, and does not know, and has no impression of the other; nor can he think that one thing which he does not know is another thing which he does not know, or that what he does not know is what he knows; nor (2) that one thing which he perceives is another thing which he perceives, or that something which he perceives is something which he does not perceive; or that something which he does not perceive is something else which he does not perceive; or that something which he does not perceive is something which he perceives; nor again (3) can he think that something which he knows and perceives, and of which he has the impression coinciding with sense, is something else which he knows and perceives, and of which he has the impression coinciding with sense;—this last case, if possible, is still more inconceivable than the others; nor (4) can he think that something which he knows and perceives, and of which he has the memorial coinciding with sense, is something else which he knows; nor so long as these agree, can he think that a thing which he knows and perceives is another thing which he perceives; or that a thing which he does not know and does not perceive, is the same as another thing which he does not know and does not perceive;—nor again, can he suppose that a thing which he does not know and does not perceive is the same as another thing which he does not know; or that a thing which he does not know and does not perceive is another thing which he does not perceive: —All these utterly and absolutely exclude the possibility of false opinion.</div><div class="sentence"> The only cases, if any, which remain, are the following.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">685</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What are they?</div><div class="sentence"> If you tell me, I may perhaps understand you better; but at present I am unable to follow you.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">686</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  A person may think that some things which he knows, or which he perceives and does not know, are some other things which he knows and perceives; or that some things which he knows and perceives, are other things which he knows and perceives.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">687</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I understand you less than ever now.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">688</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Hear me once more, then: —I, knowing Theodorus, and remembering in my own mind what sort of person he is, and also what sort of person Theaetetus is, at one time see them, and at another time do not see them, and sometimes I touch them, and at another time not, or at one time I may hear them or perceive them in some other way, and at another time not perceive them, but still I remember them, and know them in my own mind.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">689</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">690</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then, first of all, I want you to understand that a man may or may not perceive sensibly that which he knows.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">691</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">692</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And that which he does not know will sometimes not be perceived by him and sometimes will be perceived and only perceived?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">693</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  That is also true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">694</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  See whether you can follow me better now:  Socrates can recognize Theodorus and Theaetetus, but he sees neither of them, nor does he perceive them in any other way; he cannot then by any possibility imagine in his own mind that Theaetetus is Theodorus.</div><div class="sentence"> Am I not right?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">695</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  You are quite right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">696</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then that was the first case of which I spoke.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">697</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">698</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The second case was, that I, knowing one of you and not knowing the other, and perceiving neither, can never think him whom I know to be him whom I do not know.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">699</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">700</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In the third case, not knowing and not perceiving either of you, I cannot think that one of you whom I do not know is the other whom I do not know.</div><div class="sentence"> I need not again go over the catalogue of excluded cases, in which I cannot form a false opinion about you and Theodorus, either when I know both or when I am in ignorance of both, or when I know one and not the other.</div><div class="sentence"> And the same of perceiving:  do you understand me?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">701</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I do.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">702</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The only possibility of erroneous opinion is, when knowing you and Theodorus, and having on the waxen block the impression of both of you given as by a seal, but seeing you imperfectly and at a distance, I try to assign the right impression of memory to the right visual impression, and to fit this into its own print:  if I succeed, recognition will take place; but if I fail and transpose them, putting the foot into the wrong shoe—that is to say, putting the vision of either of you on to the wrong impression, or if my mind, like the sight in a mirror, which is transferred from right to left, err by reason of some similar affection, then 'heterodoxy' and false opinion ensues.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">703</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, Socrates, you have described the nature of opinion with wonderful exactness.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">704</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Or again, when I know both of you, and perceive as well as know one of you, but not the other, and my knowledge of him does not accord with perception—that was the case put by me just now which you did not understand.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">705</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  No, I did not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">706</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I meant to say, that when a person knows and perceives one of you, his knowledge coincides with his perception, he will never think him to be some other person, whom he knows and perceives, and the knowledge of whom coincides with his perception—for that also was a case supposed.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">707</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">708</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But there was an omission of the further case, in which, as we now say, false opinion may arise, when knowing both, and seeing, or having some other sensible perception of both, I fail in holding the seal over against the corresponding sensation; like a bad archer, I miss and fall wide of the mark—and this is called falsehood.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">709</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes; it is rightly so called.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">710</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  When, therefore, perception is present to one of the seals or impressions but not to the other, and the mind fits the seal of the absent perception on the one which is present, in any case of this sort the mind is deceived; in a word, if our view is sound, there can be no error or deception about things which a man does not know and has never perceived, but only in things which are known and perceived; in these alone opinion turns and twists about, and becomes alternately true and false;—true when the seals and impressions of sense meet straight and opposite—false when they go awry and crooked.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">711</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  And is not that, Socrates, nobly said?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">712</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Nobly!</div><div class="sentence"> yes; but wait a little and hear the explanation, and then you will say so with more reason; for to think truly is noble and to be deceived is base.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">713</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Undoubtedly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">714</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the origin of truth and error is as follows: —When the wax in the soul of any one is deep and abundant, and smooth and perfectly tempered, then the impressions which pass through the senses and sink into the heart of the soul, as Homer says in a parable, meaning to indicate the likeness of the soul to wax (Kerh Kerhos); these, I say, being pure and clear, and having a sufficient depth of wax, are also lasting, and minds, such as these, easily learn and easily retain, and are not liable to confusion, but have true thoughts, for they have plenty of room, and having clear impressions of things, as we term them, quickly distribute them into their proper places on the block.</div><div class="sentence"> And such men are called wise.</div><div class="sentence"> Do you agree?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">715</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Entirely.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">716</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But when the heart of any one is shaggy—a quality which the all-wise poet commends, or muddy and of impure wax, or very soft, or very hard, then there is a corresponding defect in the mind—the soft are good at learning, but apt to forget; and the hard are the reverse; the shaggy and rugged and gritty, or those who have an admixture of earth or dung in their composition, have the impressions indistinct, as also the hard, for there is no depth in them; and the soft too are indistinct, for their impressions are easily confused and effaced.</div><div class="sentence"> Yet greater is the indistinctness when they are all jostled together in a little soul, which has no room.</div><div class="sentence"> These are the natures which have false opinion; for when they see or hear or think of anything, they are slow in assigning the right objects to the right impressions—in their stupidity they confuse them, and are apt to see and hear and think amiss—and such men are said to be deceived in their knowledge of objects, and ignorant.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">717</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  No man, Socrates, can say anything truer than that.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">718</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then now we may admit the existence of false opinion in us?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">719</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">720</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And of true opinion also?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">721</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">722</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  We have at length satisfactorily proven beyond a doubt there are these two sorts of opinion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">723</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Undoubtedly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">724</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Alas, Theaetetus, what a tiresome creature is a man who is fond of talking!</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">725</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What makes you say so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">726</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Because I am disheartened at my own stupidity and tiresome garrulity; for what other term will describe the habit of a man who is always arguing on all sides of a question; whose dulness cannot be convinced, and who will never leave off?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">727</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  But what puts you out of heart?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">728</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I am not only out of heart, but in positive despair; for I do not know what to answer if any one were to ask me: —O Socrates, have you indeed discovered that false opinion arises neither in the comparison of perceptions with one another nor yet in thought, but in union of thought and perception?</div><div class="sentence"> Yes, I shall say, with the complacence of one who thinks that he has made a noble discovery.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">729</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I see no reason why we should be ashamed of our demonstration, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">730</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  He will say:  You mean to argue that the man whom we only think of and do not see, cannot be confused with the horse which we do not see or touch, but only think of and do not perceive?</div><div class="sentence"> That I believe to be my meaning, I shall reply.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">731</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Quite right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">732</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, then, he will say, according to that argument, the number eleven, which is only thought, can never be mistaken for twelve, which is only thought:  How would you answer him?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">733</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should say that a mistake may very likely arise between the eleven or twelve which are seen or handled, but that no similar mistake can arise between the eleven and twelve which are in the mind.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">734</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, but do you think that no one ever put before his own mind five and seven,—I do not mean five or seven men or horses, but five or seven in the abstract, which, as we say, are recorded on the waxen block, and in which false opinion is held to be impossible; did no man ever ask himself how many these numbers make when added together, and answer that they are eleven, while another thinks that they are twelve, or would all agree in thinking and saying that they are twelve?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">735</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not; many would think that they are eleven, and in the higher numbers the chance of error is greater still; for I assume you to be speaking of numbers in general.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">736</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Exactly; and I want you to consider whether this does not imply that the twelve in the waxen block are supposed to be eleven?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">737</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, that seems to be the case.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">738</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then do we not come back to the old difficulty?</div><div class="sentence"> For he who makes such a mistake does think one thing which he knows to be another thing which he knows; but this, as we said, was impossible, and afforded an irresistible proof of the non-existence of false opinion, because otherwise the same person would inevitably know and not know the same thing at the same time.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">739</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Most true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">740</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then false opinion cannot be explained as a confusion of thought and sense, for in that case we could not have been mistaken about pure conceptions of thought; and thus we are obliged to say, either that false opinion does not exist, or that a man may not know that which he knows;—which alternative do you prefer?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">741</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  It is hard to determine, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">742</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And yet the argument will scarcely admit of both.</div><div class="sentence"> But, as we are at our wits' end, suppose that we do a shameless thing?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">743</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What is it?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">744</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let us attempt to explain the verb 'to know.'</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">745</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  And why should that be shameless?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">746</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You seem not to be aware that the whole of our discussion from the very beginning has been a search after knowledge, of which we are assumed not to know the nature.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">747</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Nay, but I am well aware.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">748</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is it not shameless when we do not know what knowledge is, to be explaining the verb 'to know'?</div><div class="sentence"> The truth is, Theaetetus, that we have long been infected with logical impurity.</div><div class="sentence"> Thousands of times have we repeated the words 'we know,' and 'do not know,' and 'we have or have not science or knowledge,' as if we could understand what we are saying to one another, so long as we remain ignorant about knowledge; and at this moment we are using the words 'we understand,' 'we are ignorant,' as though we could still employ them when deprived of knowledge or science.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">749</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  But if you avoid these expressions, Socrates, how will you ever argue at all?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">750</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I could not, being the man I am.</div><div class="sentence"> The case would be different if I were a true hero of dialectic:  and O that such an one were present!</div><div class="sentence"> for he would have told us to avoid the use of these terms; at the same time he would not have spared in you and me the faults which I have noted.</div><div class="sentence"> But, seeing that we are no great wits, shall I venture to say what knowing is?</div><div class="sentence"> for I think that the attempt may be worth making.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">751</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Then by all means venture, and no one shall find fault with you for using the forbidden terms.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">752</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You have heard the common explanation of the verb 'to know'?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">753</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I think so, but I do not remember it at the moment.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">754</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  They explain the word 'to know' as meaning 'to have knowledge.'</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">755</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">756</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I should like to make a slight change, and say 'to possess' knowledge.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">757</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  How do the two expressions differ?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">758</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Perhaps there may be no difference; but still I should like you to hear my view, that you may help me to test it.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">759</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I will, if I can.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">760</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I should distinguish 'having' from 'possessing':  for example, a man may buy and keep under his control a garment which he does not wear; and then we should say, not that he has, but that he possesses the garment.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">761</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  It would be the correct expression.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">762</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, may not a man 'possess' and yet not 'have' knowledge in the sense of which I am speaking?</div><div class="sentence"> As you may suppose a man to have caught wild birds—doves or any other birds—and to be keeping them in an aviary which he has constructed at home; we might say of him in one sense, that he always has them because he possesses them, might we not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">763</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">764</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And yet, in another sense, he has none of them; but they are in his power, and he has got them under his hand in an enclosure of his own, and can take and have them whenever he likes;—he can catch any which he likes, and let the bird go again, and he may do so as often as he pleases.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">765</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">766</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Once more, then, as in what preceded we made a sort of waxen figment in the mind, so let us now suppose that in the mind of each man there is an aviary of all sorts of birds—some flocking together apart from the rest, others in small groups, others solitary, flying anywhere and everywhere.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">767</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Let us imagine such an aviary—and what is to follow?</div></div>
