<div class="speech"><span class="ref">768</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  We may suppose that the birds are kinds of knowledge, and that when we were children, this receptacle was empty; whenever a man has gotten and detained in the enclosure a kind of knowledge, he may be said to have learned or discovered the thing which is the subject of the knowledge:  and this is to know.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">769</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Granted.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">770</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And further, when any one wishes to catch any of these knowledges or sciences, and having taken, to hold it, and again to let them go, how will he express himself?—will he describe the 'catching' of them and the original 'possession' in the same words?</div><div class="sentence"> I will make my meaning clearer by an example: —You admit that there is an art of arithmetic?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">771</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  To be sure.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">772</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Conceive this under the form of a hunt after the science of odd and even in general.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">773</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I follow.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">774</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Having the use of the art, the arithmetician, if I am not mistaken, has the conceptions of number under his hand, and can transmit them to another.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">775</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">776</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And when transmitting them he may be said to teach them, and when receiving to learn them, and when receiving to learn them, and when having them in possession in the aforesaid aviary he may be said to know them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">777</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Exactly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">778</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Attend to what follows:  must not the perfect arithmetician know all numbers, for he has the science of all numbers in his mind?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">779</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">780</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And he can reckon abstract numbers in his head, or things about him which are numerable?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">781</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of course he can.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">782</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And to reckon is simply to consider how much such and such a number amounts to?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">783</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">784</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And so he appears to be searching into something which he knows, as if he did not know it, for we have already admitted that he knows all numbers;—you have heard these perplexing questions raised?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">785</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I have.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">786</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  May we not pursue the image of the doves, and say that the chase after knowledge is of two kinds?</div><div class="sentence"> one kind is prior to possession and for the sake of possession, and the other for the sake of taking and holding in the hands that which is possessed already.</div><div class="sentence"> And thus, when a man has learned and known something long ago, he may resume and get hold of the knowledge which he has long possessed, but has not at hand in his mind.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">787</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">788</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That was my reason for asking how we ought to speak when an arithmetician sets about numbering, or a grammarian about reading?</div><div class="sentence"> Shall we say, that although he knows, he comes back to himself to learn what he already knows?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">789</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  It would be too absurd, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">790</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Shall we say then that he is going to read or number what he does not know, although we have admitted that he knows all letters and all numbers?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">791</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  That, again, would be an absurdity.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">792</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then shall we say that about names we care nothing?—any one may twist and turn the words 'knowing' and 'learning' in any way which he likes, but since we have determined that the possession of knowledge is not the having or using it, we do assert that a man cannot not possess that which he possesses; and, therefore, in no case can a man not know that which he knows, but he may get a false opinion about it; for he may have the knowledge, not of this particular thing, but of some other;—when the various numbers and forms of knowledge are flying about in the aviary, and wishing to capture a certain sort of knowledge out of the general store, he takes the wrong one by mistake, that is to say, when he thought eleven to be twelve, he got hold of the ring-dove which he had in his mind, when he wanted the pigeon.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">793</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  A very rational explanation.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">794</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But when he catches the one which he wants, then he is not deceived, and has an opinion of what is, and thus false and true opinion may exist, and the difficulties which were previously raised disappear.</div><div class="sentence"> I dare say that you agree with me, do you not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">795</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">796</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And so we are rid of the difficulty of a man's not knowing what he knows, for we are not driven to the inference that he does not possess what he possesses, whether he be or be not deceived.</div><div class="sentence"> And yet I fear that a greater difficulty is looking in at the window.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">797</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What is it?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">798</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  How can the exchange of one knowledge for another ever become false opinion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">799</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">800</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In the first place, how can a man who has the knowledge of anything be ignorant of that which he knows, not by reason of ignorance, but by reason of his own knowledge?</div><div class="sentence"> And, again, is it not an extreme absurdity that he should suppose another thing to be this, and this to be another thing;—that, having knowledge present with him in his mind, he should still know nothing and be ignorant of all things?—you might as well argue that ignorance may make a man know, and blindness make him see, as that knowledge can make him ignorant.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">801</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Perhaps, Socrates, we may have been wrong in making only forms of knowledge our birds:  whereas there ought to have been forms of ignorance as well, flying about together in the mind, and then he who sought to take one of them might sometimes catch a form of knowledge, and sometimes a form of ignorance; and thus he would have a false opinion from ignorance, but a true one from knowledge, about the same thing.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">802</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I cannot help praising you, Theaetetus, and yet I must beg you to reconsider your words.</div><div class="sentence"> Let us grant what you say—then, according to you, he who takes ignorance will have a false opinion—am I right?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">803</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">804</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  He will certainly not think that he has a false opinion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">805</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of course not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">806</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  He will think that his opinion is true, and he will fancy that he knows the things about which he has been deceived?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">807</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">808</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then he will think that he has captured knowledge and not ignorance?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">809</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">810</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And thus, after going a long way round, we are once more face to face with our original difficulty.</div><div class="sentence"> The hero of dialectic will retort upon us: —'O my excellent friends, he will say, laughing, if a man knows the form of ignorance and the form of knowledge, can he think that one of them which he knows is the other which he knows?</div><div class="sentence"> or, if he knows neither of them, can he think that the one which he knows not is another which he knows not?</div><div class="sentence"> or, if he knows one and not the other, can he think the one which he knows to be the one which he does not know?</div><div class="sentence"> or the one which he does not know to be the one which he knows?</div><div class="sentence"> or will you tell me that there are other forms of knowledge which distinguish the right and wrong birds, and which the owner keeps in some other aviaries or graven on waxen blocks according to your foolish images, and which he may be said to know while he possesses them, even though he have them not at hand in his mind?</div><div class="sentence"> And thus, in a perpetual circle, you will be compelled to go round and round, and you will make no progress.'</div><div class="sentence"> What are we to say in reply, Theaetetus?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">811</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Indeed, Socrates, I do not know what we are to say.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">812</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Are not his reproaches just, and does not the argument truly show that we are wrong in seeking for false opinion until we know what knowledge is; that must be first ascertained; then, the nature of false opinion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">813</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I cannot but agree with you, Socrates, so far as we have yet gone.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">814</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then, once more, what shall we say that knowledge is?—for we are not going to lose heart as yet.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">815</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly, I shall not lose heart, if you do not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">816</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What definition will be most consistent with our former views?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">817</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I cannot think of any but our old one, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">818</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What was it?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">819</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Knowledge was said by us to be true opinion; and true opinion is surely unerring, and the results which follow from it are all noble and good.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">820</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  He who led the way into the river, Theaetetus, said 'The experiment will show;' and perhaps if we go forward in the search, we may stumble upon the thing which we are looking for; but if we stay where we are, nothing will come to light.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">821</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true; let us go forward and try.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">822</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The trail soon comes to an end, for a whole profession is against us.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">823</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  How is that, and what profession do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">824</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The profession of the great wise ones who are called orators and lawyers; for these persuade men by their art and make them think whatever they like, but they do not teach them.</div><div class="sentence"> Do you imagine that there are any teachers in the world so clever as to be able to convince others of the truth about acts of robbery or violence, of which they were not eye-witnesses, while a little water is flowing in the clepsydra?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">825</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not, they can only persuade them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">826</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And would you not say that persuading them is making them have an opinion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">827</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  To be sure.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">828</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  When, therefore, judges are justly persuaded about matters which you can know only by seeing them, and not in any other way, and when thus judging of them from report they attain a true opinion about them, they judge without knowledge, and yet are rightly persuaded, if they have judged well.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">829</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">830</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And yet, O my friend, if true opinion in law courts and knowledge are the same, the perfect judge could not have judged rightly without knowledge; and therefore I must infer that they are not the same.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">831</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  That is a distinction, Socrates, which I have heard made by some one else, but I had forgotten it.</div><div class="sentence"> He said that true opinion, combined with reason, was knowledge, but that the opinion which had no reason was out of the sphere of knowledge; and that things of which there is no rational account are not knowable—such was the singular expression which he used—and that things which have a reason or explanation are knowable.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">832</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Excellent; but then, how did he distinguish between things which are and are not 'knowable'?</div><div class="sentence"> I wish that you would repeat to me what he said, and then I shall know whether you and I have heard the same tale.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">833</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I do not know whether I can recall it; but if another person would tell me, I think that I could follow him.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">834</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let me give you, then, a dream in return for a dream: —Methought that I too had a dream, and I heard in my dream that the primeval letters or elements out of which you and I and all other things are compounded, have no reason or explanation; you can only name them, but no predicate can be either affirmed or denied of them, for in the one case existence, in the other non-existence is already implied, neither of which must be added, if you mean to speak of this or that thing by itself alone.</div><div class="sentence"> It should not be called itself, or that, or each, or alone, or this, or the like; for these go about everywhere and are applied to all things, but are distinct from them; whereas, if the first elements could be described, and had a definition of their own, they would be spoken of apart from all else.</div><div class="sentence"> But none of these primeval elements can be defined; they can only be named, for they have nothing but a name, and the things which are compounded of them, as they are complex, are expressed by a combination of names, for the combination of names is the essence of a definition.</div><div class="sentence"> Thus, then, the elements or letters are only objects of perception, and cannot be defined or known; but the syllables or combinations of them are known and expressed, and are apprehended by true opinion.</div><div class="sentence"> When, therefore, any one forms the true opinion of anything without rational explanation, you may say that his mind is truly exercised, but has no knowledge; for he who cannot give and receive a reason for a thing, has no knowledge of that thing; but when he adds rational explanation, then, he is perfected in knowledge and may be all that I have been denying of him.</div><div class="sentence"> Was that the form in which the dream appeared to you?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">835</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Precisely.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">836</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And you allow and maintain that true opinion, combined with definition or rational explanation, is knowledge?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">837</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Exactly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">838</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then may we assume, Theaetetus, that to-day, and in this casual manner, we have found a truth which in former times many wise men have grown old and have not found?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">839</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  At any rate, Socrates, I am satisfied with the present statement.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">840</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Which is probably correct—for how can there be knowledge apart from definition and true opinion?</div><div class="sentence"> And yet there is one point in what has been said which does not quite satisfy me.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">841</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What was it?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">842</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What might seem to be the most ingenious notion of all: —That the elements or letters are unknown, but the combination or syllables known.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">843</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  And was that wrong?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">844</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  We shall soon know; for we have as hostages the instances which the author of the argument himself used.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">845</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What hostages?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">846</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The letters, which are the clements; and the syllables, which are the combinations;—he reasoned, did he not, from the letters of the alphabet?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">847</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes; he did.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">848</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let us take them and put them to the test, or rather, test ourselves: —What was the way in which we learned letters?</div><div class="sentence"> and, first of all, are we right in saying that syllables have a definition, but that letters have no definition?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">849</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I think so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">850</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I think so too; for, suppose that some one asks you to spell the first syllable of my name: —Theaetetus, he says, what is SO?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">851</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should reply S and O.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">852</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That is the definition which you would give of the syllable?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">853</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">854</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I wish that you would give me a similar definition of the S.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">855</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  But how can any one, Socrates, tell the elements of an element?</div><div class="sentence"> I can only reply, that S is a consonant, a mere noise, as of the tongue hissing; B, and most other letters, again, are neither vowel-sounds nor noises.</div><div class="sentence"> Thus letters may be most truly said to be undefined; for even the most distinct of them, which are the seven vowels, have a sound only, but no definition at all.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">856</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then, I suppose, my friend, that we have been so far right in our idea about knowledge?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">857</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes; I think that we have.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">858</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, but have we been right in maintaining that the syllables can be known, but not the letters?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">859</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I think so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">860</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And do we mean by a syllable two letters, or if there are more, all of them, or a single idea which arises out of the combination of them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">861</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should say that we mean all the letters.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">862</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Take the case of the two letters S and O, which form the first syllable of my own name; must not he who knows the syllable, know both of them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">863</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">864</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  He knows, that is, the S and O?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">865</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">866</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But can he be ignorant of either singly and yet know both together?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">867</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Such a supposition, Socrates, is monstrous and unmeaning.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">868</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But if he cannot know both without knowing each, then if he is ever to know the syllable, he must know the letters first; and thus the fine theory has again taken wings and departed.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">869</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, with wonderful celerity.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">870</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, we did not keep watch properly.</div><div class="sentence"> Perhaps we ought to have maintained that a syllable is not the letters, but rather one single idea framed out of them, having a separate form distinct from them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">871</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true; and a more likely notion than the other.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">872</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Take care; let us not be cowards and betray a great and imposing theory.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">873</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  No, indeed.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">874</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let us assume then, as we now say, that the syllable is a simple form arising out of the several combinations of harmonious elements—of letters or of any other elements.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">875</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very good.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">876</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And it must have no parts.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">877</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Why?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">878</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Because that which has parts must be a whole of all the parts.</div><div class="sentence"> Or would you say that a whole, although formed out of the parts, is a single notion different from all the parts?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">879</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">880</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And would you say that all and the whole are the same, or different?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">881</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I am not certain; but, as you like me to answer at once, I shall hazard the reply, that they are different.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">882</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I approve of your readiness, Theaetetus, but I must take time to think whether I equally approve of your answer.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">883</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes; the answer is the point.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">884</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  According to this new view, the whole is supposed to differ from all?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">885</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">886</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, but is there any difference between all (in the plural) and the all (in the singular)?</div><div class="sentence"> Take the case of number: —When we say one, two, three, four, five, six; or when we say twice three, or three times two, or four and two, or three and two and one, are we speaking of the same or of different numbers?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">887</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of the same.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">888</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That is of six?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">889</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">890</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And in each form of expression we spoke of all the six?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">891</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">892</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Again, in speaking of all (in the plural) is there not one thing which we express?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">893</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Of course there is.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">894</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And that is six?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">895</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">896</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then in predicating the word 'all' of things measured by number, we predicate at the same time a singular and a plural?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">897</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly we do.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">898</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Again, the number of the acre and the acre are the same; are they not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">899</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">900</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the number of the stadium in like manner is the stadium?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">901</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">902</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the army is the number of the army; and in all similar cases, the entire number of anything is the entire thing?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">903</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">904</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the number of each is the parts of each?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">905</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Exactly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">906</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then as many things as have parts are made up of parts?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">907</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">908</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But all the parts are admitted to be the all, if the entire number is the all?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">909</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">910</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then the whole is not made up of parts, for it would be the all, if consisting of all the parts?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">911</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  That is the inference.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">912</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But is a part a part of anything but the whole?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">913</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, of the all.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">914</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You make a valiant defence, Theaetetus.</div><div class="sentence"> And yet is not the all that of which nothing is wanting?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">915</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">916</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is not a whole likewise that from which nothing is absent?</div><div class="sentence"> but that from which anything is absent is neither a whole nor all;—if wanting in anything, both equally lose their entirety of nature.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">917</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I now think that there is no difference between a whole and all.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">918</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But were we not saying that when a thing has parts, all the parts will be a whole and all?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">919</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">920</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then, as I was saying before, must not the alternative be that either the syllable is not the letters, and then the letters are not parts of the syllable, or that the syllable will be the same with the letters, and will therefore be equally known with them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">921</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  You are right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">922</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And, in order to avoid this, we suppose it to be different from them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">923</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">924</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But if letters are not parts of syllables, can you tell me of any other parts of syllables, which are not letters?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">925</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  No, indeed, Socrates; for if I admit the existence of parts in a syllable, it would be ridiculous in me to give up letters and seek for other parts.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">926</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Quite true, Theaetetus, and therefore, according to our present view, a syllable must surely be some indivisible form?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">927</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">928</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But do you remember, my friend, that only a little while ago we admitted and approved the statement, that of the first elements out of which all other things are compounded there could be no definition, because each of them when taken by itself is uncompounded; nor can one rightly attribute to them the words 'being' or 'this,' because they are alien and inappropriate words, and for this reason the letters or elements were indefinable and unknown?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">929</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I remember.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">930</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is not this also the reason why they are simple and indivisible?</div><div class="sentence"> I can see no other.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">931</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  No other reason can be given.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">932</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then is not the syllable in the same case as the elements or letters, if it has no parts and is one form?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">933</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  To be sure.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">934</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  If, then, a syllable is a whole, and has many parts or letters, the letters as well as the syllable must be intelligible and expressible, since all the parts are acknowledged to be the same as the whole?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">935</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">936</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But if it be one and indivisible, then the syllables and the letters are alike undefined and unknown, and for the same reason?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">937</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I cannot deny that.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">938</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  We cannot, therefore, agree in the opinion of him who says that the syllable can be known and expressed, but not the letters.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">939</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not; if we may trust the argument.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">940</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, but will you not be equally inclined to disagree with him, when you remember your own experience in learning to read?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">941</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What experience?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">942</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Why, that in learning you were kept trying to distinguish the separate letters both by the eye and by the ear, in order that, when you heard them spoken or saw them written, you might not be confused by their position.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">943</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">944</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is the education of the harp-player complete unless he can tell what string answers to a particular note; the notes, as every one would allow, are the elements or letters of music?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">945</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Exactly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">946</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then, if we argue from the letters and syllables which we know to other simples and compounds, we shall say that the letters or simple elements as a class are much more certainly known than the syllables, and much more indispensable to a perfect knowledge of any subject; and if some one says that the syllable is known and the letter unknown, we shall consider that either intentionally or unintentionally he is talking nonsense?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">947</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Exactly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">948</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And there might be given other proofs of this belief, if I am not mistaken.</div><div class="sentence"> But do not let us in looking for them lose sight of the question before us, which is the meaning of the statement, that right opinion with rational definition or explanation is the most perfect form of knowledge.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">949</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  We must not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">950</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, and what is the meaning of the term 'explanation'?</div><div class="sentence"> I think that we have a choice of three meanings.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">951</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What are they?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">952</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In the first place, the meaning may be, manifesting one's thought by the voice with verbs and nouns, imaging an opinion in the stream which flows from the lips, as in a mirror or water.</div><div class="sentence"> Does not explanation appear to be of this nature?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">953</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly; he who so manifests his thought, is said to explain himself.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">954</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And every one who is not born deaf or dumb is able sooner or later to manifest what he thinks of anything; and if so, all those who have a right opinion about anything will also have right explanation; nor will right opinion be anywhere found to exist apart from knowledge.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">955</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">956</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let us not, therefore, hastily charge him who gave this account of knowledge with uttering an unmeaning word; for perhaps he only intended to say, that when a person was asked what was the nature of anything, he should be able to answer his questioner by giving the elements of the thing.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">957</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  As for example, Socrates...?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">958</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  As, for example, when Hesiod says that a waggon is made up of a hundred planks.</div><div class="sentence"> Now, neither you nor I could describe all of them individually; but if any one asked what is a waggon, we should be content to answer, that a waggon consists of wheels, axle, body, rims, yoke.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">959</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">960</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And our opponent will probably laugh at us, just as he would if we professed to be grammarians and to give a grammatical account of the name of Theaetetus, and yet could only tell the syllables and not the letters of your name—that would be true opinion, and not knowledge; for knowledge, as has been already remarked, is not attained until, combined with true opinion, there is an enumeration of the elements out of which anything is composed.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">961</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">962</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In the same general way, we might also have true opinion about a waggon; but he who can describe its essence by an enumeration of the hundred planks, adds rational explanation to true opinion, and instead of opinion has art and knowledge of the nature of a waggon, in that he attains to the whole through the elements.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">963</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  And do you not agree in that view, Socrates?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">964</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  If you do, my friend; but I want to know first, whether you admit the resolution of all things into their elements to be a rational explanation of them, and the consideration of them in syllables or larger combinations of them to be irrational—is this your view?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">965</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Precisely.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">966</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, and do you conceive that a man has knowledge of any element who at one time affirms and at another time denies that element of something, or thinks that the same thing is composed of different elements at different times?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">967</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Assuredly not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">968</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And do you not remember that in your case and in that of others this often occurred in the process of learning to read?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">969</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  You mean that I mistook the letters and misspelt the syllables?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">970</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">971</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  To be sure; I perfectly remember, and I am very far from supposing that they who are in this condition have knowledge.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">972</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  When a person at the time of learning writes the name of Theaetetus, and thinks that he ought to write and does write Th and e; but, again, meaning to write the name of Theododorus, thinks that he ought to write and does write T and e—can we suppose that he knows the first syllables of your two names?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">973</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  We have already admitted that such a one has not yet attained knowledge.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">974</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And in like manner be may enumerate without knowing them the second and third and fourth syllables of your name?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">975</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  He may.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">976</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And in that case, when he knows the order of the letters and can write them out correctly, he has right opinion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">977</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">978</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But although we admit that he has right opinion, he will still be without knowledge?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">979</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">980</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And yet he will have explanation, as well as right opinion, for he knew the order of the letters when he wrote; and this we admit to be explanation.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">981</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">982</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then, my friend, there is such a thing as right opinion united with definition or explanation, which does not as yet attain to the exactness of knowledge.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">983</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  It would seem so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">984</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And what we fancied to be a perfect definition of knowledge is a dream only.</div><div class="sentence"> But perhaps we had better not say so as yet, for were there not three explanations of knowledge, one of which must, as we said, be adopted by him who maintains knowledge to be true opinion combined with rational explanation?</div><div class="sentence"> And very likely there may be found some one who will not prefer this but the third.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">985</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  You are quite right; there is still one remaining.</div><div class="sentence"> The first was the image or expression of the mind in speech; the second, which has just been mentioned, is a way of reaching the whole by an enumeration of the elements.</div><div class="sentence"> But what is the third definition?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">986</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  There is, further, the popular notion of telling the mark or sign of difference which distinguishes the thing in question from all others.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">987</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Can you give me any example of such a definition?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">988</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  As, for example, in the case of the sun, I think that you would be contented with the statement that the sun is the brightest of the heavenly bodies which revolve about the earth.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">989</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">990</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Understand why: —the reason is, as I was just now saying, that if you get at the difference and distinguishing characteristic of each thing, then, as many persons affirm, you will get at the definition or explanation of it; but while you lay hold only of the common and not of the characteristic notion, you will only have the definition of those things to which this common quality belongs.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">991</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I understand you, and your account of definition is in my judgment correct.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">992</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But he, who having right opinion about anything, can find out the difference which distinguishes it from other things will know that of which before he had only an opinion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">993</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes; that is what we are maintaining.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">994</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Nevertheless, Theaetetus, on a nearer view, I find myself quite disappointed; the picture, which at a distance was not so bad, has now become altogether unintelligible.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">995</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">996</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I will endeavour to explain:  I will suppose myself to have true opinion of you, and if to this I add your definition, then I have knowledge, but if not, opinion only.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">997</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">998</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The definition was assumed to be the interpretation of your difference.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">999</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1000</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But when I had only opinion, I had no conception of your distinguishing characteristics.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1001</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I suppose not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1002</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then I must have conceived of some general or common nature which no more belonged to you than to another.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1003</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1004</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Tell me, now—How in that case could I have formed a judgment of you any more than of any one else?</div><div class="sentence"> Suppose that I imagine Theaetetus to be a man who has nose, eyes, and mouth, and every other member complete; how would that enable me to distinguish Theaetetus from Theodorus, or from some outer barbarian?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1005</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  How could it?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1006</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Or if I had further conceived of you, not only as having nose and eyes, but as having a snub nose and prominent eyes, should I have any more notion of you than of myself and others who resemble me?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1007</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1008</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Surely I can have no conception of Theaetetus until your snub-nosedness has left an impression on my mind different from the snub-nosedness of all others whom I have ever seen, and until your other peculiarities have a like distinctness; and so when I meet you to-morrow the right opinion will be re-called?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1009</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Most true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1010</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then right opinion implies the perception of differences?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1011</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1012</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What, then, shall we say of adding reason or explanation to right opinion?</div><div class="sentence"> If the meaning is, that we should form an opinion of the way in which something differs from another thing, the proposal is ridiculous.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1013</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  How so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1014</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  We are supposed to acquire a right opinion of the differences which distinguish one thing from another when we have already a right opinion of them, and so we go round and round: —the revolution of the scytal, or pestle, or any other rotatory machine, in the same circles, is as nothing compared with such a requirement; and we may be truly described as the blind directing the blind; for to add those things which we already have, in order that we may learn what we already think, is like a soul utterly benighted.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1015</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Tell me; what were you going to say just now, when you asked the question?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1016</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  If, my boy, the argument, in speaking of adding the definition, had used the word to 'know,' and not merely 'have an opinion' of the difference, this which is the most promising of all the definitions of knowledge would have come to a pretty end, for to know is surely to acquire knowledge.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1017</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1018</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And so, when the question is asked, What is knowledge?</div><div class="sentence"> this fair argument will answer 'Right opinion with knowledge,'—knowledge, that is, of difference, for this, as the said argument maintains, is adding the definition.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1019</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  That seems to be true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1020</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But how utterly foolish, when we are asking what is knowledge, that the reply should only be, right opinion with knowledge of difference or of anything!</div><div class="sentence"> And so, Theaetetus, knowledge is neither sensation nor true opinion, nor yet definition and explanation accompanying and added to true opinion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1021</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I suppose not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1022</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And are you still in labour and travail, my dear friend, or have you brought all that you have to say about knowledge to the birth?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1023</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  I am sure, Socrates, that you have elicited from me a good deal more than ever was in me.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1024</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And does not my art show that you have brought forth wind, and that the offspring of your brain are not worth bringing up?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1025</span><div class="speaker">THEAETETUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1026</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But if, Theaetetus, you should ever conceive afresh, you will be all the better for the present investigation, and if not, you will be soberer and humbler and gentler to other men, and will be too modest to fancy that you know what you do not know.</div><div class="sentence"> These are the limits of my art; I can no further go, nor do I know aught of the things which great and famous men know or have known in this or former ages.</div><div class="sentence"> The office of a midwife I, like my mother, have received from God; she delivered women, I deliver men; but they must be young and noble and fair.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">1027</span> <div class="sentence">  And now I have to go to the porch of the King Archon, where I am to meet Meletus and his indictment.</div><div class="sentence"> To-morrow morning, Theodorus, I shall hope to see you again at this place.</div></div>

</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
